Wright v. Commonwealth


2011-SC-000191-MR


SUPREME COURT OF KENTUCKY


391 S.W.3d 743; 2012 Ky. LEXIS 496



October 25, 2012, Rendered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication February 21, 2013. [**1] 
As Modified February 21, 2013.


PRIOR HISTORY: ON APPEAL FROM CAMPBELL CIRCUIT COURT. HONORABLE FRED A. STINE, V., JUDGE. NO. 10-CR-00177.
Wright v. Commonwealth, 391 S.W.3d 743, 2012 Ky. LEXIS 170 (Ky., 2012)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the Campbell Circuit Court (Kentucky) of first-degree fleeing or evading police, under Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 520.095, fourth-degree assault, possession of marijuana, and being a first-degree persistent felony offender (PFO 1), and he was sentenced to 20 years in prison and fined $600. He appealed his fleeing and evading conviction and his fine.


OVERVIEW: Defendant said the trial court erroneously instructed the jury on fleeing and evading. The supreme court agreed because (1) Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 520.095 required a finding that he was fleeing immediately after committing an act of domestic violence as defined in Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 403.720, requiring findings that he committed an act of violence enumerated in Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 403.720, and that he and the victim were members of an "unmarried couple," but, (2) under the instruction given, the jury had to find him guilty if it decided he and the victim had "shared living quarters," without mentioning the statutory definition of an "unmarried couple" in Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 403.720(4), so the instructions were too broad. The error was not harmless because (1) erroneous instructions were presumed prejudicial, and (2) that presumption was not rebutted when the jury was improperly instructed on the elements of the crime. It was error to impose criminal fines because (1) the trial court previously found defendant to be indigent, under Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 31.100(3), and (2) Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 534.040(4) barred the imposition of fines on a person found to be indigent.


OUTCOME: Defendant's convictions and sentences for first-degree fleeing or evading and being a PFO 1 were reversed, the matter was remanded to the trial court for further proceedings, and those portions of defendant's sentences imposing fines for fourth-degree assault and possession of marijuana were vacated.

Keeling v. Commonwealth


2010-SC-000351-MR


SUPREME COURT OF KENTUCKY


381 S.W.3d 248; 2012 Ky. LEXIS 160



October 25, 2012, Rendered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication November 15, 2012.
Post-conviction relief denied at Keeling v. Commonwealth, 2015 Ky. App. Unpub. LEXIS 777 (Ky. Ct. App., Nov. 20, 2015)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
ON APPEAL FROM GRAVES CIRCUIT COURT. HONORABLE TIMOTHY C. STARK, JUDGE. NO. 08-CR-00001.
Keeling v. Commonwealth, 2009 Ky. App. Unpub. LEXIS 1043 (Ky. Ct. App., Dec. 11, 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury found defendant guilty but mentally ill (GBMI) of murder and guilty of first-degree assault (as a lesser included offense of attempted murder). The Graves Circuit Court, Kentucky, adopted recommendations of life in prison for the murder conviction and 20 years in prison for the assault conviction. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: The Supreme Court of Kentucky held that the dismissal of a prior indictment based on incompetence to stand trial, without a designation as with or without prejudice, was effectively without prejudice; therefore, it did not act as an adjudication on the merits, and double jeopardy and res judicata issues were inapposite. In reaching that conclusion, the court held that a trial court's dismissal of an indictment based on a finding that the defendant is incompetent to stand trial is not a dismissal with prejudice unless it is designated as such with the consent of the Commonwealth's attorney. Therefore, applying Ky. R. Civ. P. 41.02(3) to criminal cases violates separation of powers principles. Further, prosecution of a criminal defendant originally found incompetent to stand trial is permissible on a subsequent finding of competency, so long as the later prosecution does not violate Constitutional rights. The court also held that the GBMI instruction was proper; there was sufficient evidence of competence; the evidence did not support an instruction on extreme emotional disturbance; defendant's mental illness did not render his statements involuntary; and joinder was proper.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the trial court.

Walker v. Blair


2012-SC-000004-DGE


SUPREME COURT OF KENTUCKY


382 S.W.3d 862; 2012 Ky. LEXIS 179



October 25, 2012, Rendered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication November 15, 2012.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
ON REVIEW FROM COURT OF APPEALS. CASE NO. 2010-CA-002228-ME. JEFFERSON CIRCUIT COURT NO. 09-CI-503800.
Walker v. Blair, 2011 Ky. App. Unpub. LEXIS 982 (Ky. Ct. App., Sept. 30, 2011)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant mother sought review of an order from the Kentucky Court of Appeals, which upheld an order granting appellee paternal grandmother's petition for visitation with the mother's child.


OVERVIEW: The mother and father had a child together. The father committed suicide. A few months later, the paternal grandmother filed a petition under Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 405.021(1) to establish grandparent visitation with the child. The mother opposed the petition and filed a motion to dismiss, which the trial court denied. The trial court found that it was in the child's best interest to grant visitation with the grandmother. The court of appeals upheld the decision. On review, the court found that in granting the grandmother visitation under § 405.021(1), the trial court failed to give the mother's decision to deny visitation the special weight that was due a fit parent. The grandmother failed to rebut by clear and convincing evidence the presumption that the mother was acting in the child's best interest. A new evidentiary hearing was appropriate because the trial court did not apply the appropriate standard in determining whether visitation with the grandmother was in the child's best interest, the trial court's findings of fact were troubling, and two years had passed since the original hearing. The trial court properly denied the mother's motion to dismiss the petition.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the decision of the court of appeals, vacated the trial court's visitation order, and remanded the case to the trial court with directions to conduct a new evidentiary hearing on the grandmother's petition.


 

73 of 1068 DOCUMENTS



Jefferson County Bd. of Educ. v. Fell


2011-SC-000658-DGE


SUPREME COURT OF KENTUCKY


391 S.W.3d 713; 2012 Ky. LEXIS 148



September 20, 2012, Rendered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication October 22, 2012.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
ON REVIEW FROM COURT OF APPEALS. CASE NO. 2010-CA-001830-MR. JEFFERSON CIRCUIT COURT NO. 10-CI-004174.
Fell v. Jefferson County Bd. of Educ., 2011 Ky. App. LEXIS 176 (Ky. Ct. App., Sept. 30, 2011)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff parents sued defendants, a county board of education and its superintendent, claiming their children were denied the statutory right afforded them by Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 159.070 to attend the public school nearest their home. The trial court granted defendants' motion to dismiss for failure to state a claim. Plaintiffs appealed; the Court of Appeals of Kentucky reversed. Defendants appealed.


OVERVIEW: Under § 159.070, parents were "permitted to enroll their children in the public school nearest their home." Plaintiffs argued that this meant their children were entitled to attend that neighborhood school, while defendants argued that "enroll" was not synonymous with "attend." The intermediate appellate court agreed with plaintiffs' construction of § 159.070, ordered defendants to develop a new student assignment plan, and granted a local circuit judge final authority on the assignment of public school students. The high court noted that the terms "enroll" and "attend" were not used interchangeably in Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. tit. XIII, ch. 159, and held that "enroll," as used § 159.070, did not carry the weight ascribed to it by plaintiffs. Based on the language of § 159.070, which was ambiguous, its legislative history, and the other provisions of ch. 159, the high court held that § 159.070 did not grant schoolchildren a right to attend the school nearest their home. The assignment of pupils to schools within a school district was a matter the legislature committed to the sound discretion of the local school board. Therefore, the trial court properly dismissed plaintiffs' complaint.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the intermediate appellate court was reversed and the judgment of the trial court was reinstated.

Commonwealth v. Abnee


2011-SC-000507-DG


SUPREME COURT OF KENTUCKY


375 S.W.3d 49; 2012 Ky. LEXIS 121



August 23, 2012, Rendered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication September 13, 2012.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
ON REVIEW FROM COURT OF APPEALS. CASE NO. 2009-CA-000749-MR. NICHOLAS CIRCUIT COURT NO. 06-CR-00017.
Abnee v. Commonwealth, 2011 Ky. App. Unpub. LEXIS 564 (Ky. Ct. App., July 29, 2011)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Commonwealth of Kentucky sought review of an order from the Kentucky Court of Appeals, which reversed an order denying defendant's motion for a new trial and remanded the case for an evidentiary hearing.


OVERVIEW: Several weeks after defendant was convicted of first-degree sodomy, he moved for a new trial based upon an unsworn, unauthenticated note purportedly written by a member of the jury panel that convicted him. The note stated that the jury had been influenced by defendant's criminal record, which was in the jury room, and that the writer had been pressured into voting to convict. The trial court denied the motion for a new trial. The court of appeals reversed and remanded the case for a hearing to ascertain the validity of the claim. On review, the court held that an unsworn letter by a lone juror, lacking authentication and verification, was insufficient under Ky. R. Crim. P. 10.04 to trigger the process for further inquiry regarding whether the jury verdict was tainted by the consideration of extrinsic information. An inquiry into the validity of the jury verdict would necessitate bringing in the entire jury for examination, along with the bailiff. Such a process would have been triggered by an unauthenticated and unsworn document written, apparently, by a person who was unwilling to replicate her allegations under oath and who would not speak to trial counsel about the allegations.


OUTCOME: The decision of the court of appeals was reversed, and the judgment of the trial court was reinstated.

Alred v. Commonwealth


2011-SC-000558-RR


SUPREME COURT OF KENTUCKY


2012 Ky. LEXIS 101



July 23, 2012, Rendered


NOTICE: TO BE PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication October 25, 2012.
Reprinted as amended at Alred v. Commonwealth, 2012 Ky. LEXIS 407 (Ky., July 23, 2012)
Modified and rehearing denied by Alred v. Commonwealth, 2012 Ky. LEXIS 406 (Ky., Oct. 25, 2012)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Movant judge sought review of the decision of respondent Judicial Conduct Commission (Commission) (Kentucky), asking the supreme court to overturn the Commission's finding of official misconduct on 9 of the 20 allegations brought against him for misconduct in office and the order that the judge be removed from office.


OVERVIEW: The judge became the focus of a lengthy investigation by the Commission, culminating in formal charges consisting of 20 allegations of misconduct in office. The commission found official misconduct on 9 of the charges and ordered that he be removed from office. The judge asked the supreme court to overturn the findings, but the supreme court affirmed as to 8 counts of official misconduct and the Commission's decision to remove the judge from office. The supreme court stated, in part, that he violated Ky. Sup. Ct. R. 4.300 by personally participating in fund-raising. Additionally, he also violated Ky. Sup. Ct. R. 4.020 and 4.300 when he voluntarily dismissed a complain against Kentucky Utilities and then urged its counsel to agree to donate money for playground equipment at the elementary school that the judge's children attended. The judge further engaged in ex parte communications and changed a defendant's bond conditions without a hearing or giving notice to that defendant.


OUTCOME: The supreme court affirmed the Commission's order as to eight counts of official misconduct and the Commission's decision to remove the judge from office. The supreme court further reversed the Commission's findings and legal conclusions as clearly erroneous as to Count V.

 

76 of 1068 DOCUMENTS


Alred v. Commonwealth


2011-SC-000558-RR


SUPREME COURT OF KENTUCKY


395 S.W.3d 417; 2012 Ky. LEXIS 407



July 23, 2012, Rendered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] 
MODIFIED: October 25, 2012.


PRIOR HISTORY: Alred v. Commonwealth, 2012 Ky. LEXIS 101 (Ky., July 23, 2012)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Respondent Kentucky Judicial Conduct Commission (Commission) brought 20 allegations of misconduct in office against movant judge. Following an adversarial hearing on these charges, the Commission found official misconduct on nine charges and ordered the judge removed from office, under Ky. Const. § 121. The judge sought review.


OVERVIEW: The judge said Ky. Sup. Ct. R. 4.300, Canons 1 and 2A, were vague. The supreme court disagreed because he knew his duties and possible discipline. His Sixth Amendment rights were not violated because it did not apply. The Commission could be investigator and adjudicator because it was an administrative body. Its members did not have to recuse because this was not required by (1) the chair's association with an investigator, (2) representation by counsel who presented evidence, (3) the chair's comments, and (4) receipt of Ky. Sup. Ct. R. 4.170 pre-hearing material. His Ky. Sup. Ct. R. 4.170 rights were observed because (1) he had complainants' identities, and (2) he was not denied exculpatory material. The Commission's findings did not clearly err as to eight counts because he (1) used his office for personal objectives, (2) made misrepresentations in an order, (3) had an official investigated before an election in which his cousin ran, (4) had ex parte communications, (5) heard cases when he was a complainant, (6) raised funds, and (7) ignored required procedures in removing counsel. Changing a defendant's release terms was not misconduct because nothing showed no hearing was held.


OUTCOME: The Commission's order was affirmed as to eight counts of official misconduct and its decision to remove the judge. The order was reversed as to one count.

Elery v. Commonwealth


2010-SC-000669-MR


SUPREME COURT OF KENTUCKY


368 S.W.3d 78; 2012 Ky. LEXIS 87



June 21, 2012, Rendered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication July 12, 2012.
Post-conviction relief denied at Elery v. Commonwealth, 2015 Ky. App. Unpub. LEXIS 400 (Ky. Ct. App., June 5, 2015)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
ON APPEAL FROM JEFFERSON CIRCUIT COURT. HONORABLE IRVIN G. MAZE, JUDGE. NO. 09-CR-000625.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Jefferson Circuit Court (Kentucky) convicted defendant of murder, tampering with physical evidence, and violating a protective order. Though the Commonwealth sought the death penalty, defendant was instead sentenced to life in prison without benefit of probation or parole. Defendant appealed to the supreme court as a matter of right under Ky. Const. § 110(2)(b).


OVERVIEW: The harmless error rule did not allow reversal for the unredacted portions of defendant's interviews with police because none of the statements raised by defendant could have had a substantial effect on the jury. Also, there was little to no risk that the jury would have considered the evidence, inferred that another crime occurred, and convicted defendant. While the statements should have been redacted from both the recording and the transcript, their inclusion amounted to harmless error. In addition, requiring the Commonwealth to prove the existence of extreme emotional disturbance (EED) for a first-degree manslaughter conviction or the absence of EED for a murder conviction was erroneous, but any error was prejudicial only to the Commonwealth, not defendant. Thus, the error was harmless. Additionally, Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 189A.104's bar on the admission of portable breathalyzer test (PBT) results in driving under the influence (DUI) cases did not bar defendant seeking to admit that result as part of a defense in a non-DUI prosecution. However, the exclusion of the PBT evidence was harmless error.


OUTCOME: The supreme court affirmed defendant's convictions and sentence.

CASE SUMMARY:

Wilson v. Commonwealth


2010-SC-000573-MR


SUPREME COURT OF KENTUCKY


381 S.W.3d 180; 2012 Ky. LEXIS 64



May 24, 2012, Rendered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication November 15, 2012.
Review denied by Wilson v. Commonwealth, 2012 Ky. LEXIS 335 (Ky., Nov. 21, 2012)
Decision reached on appeal by Wilson v. Commonwealth, 2015 Ky. Unpub. LEXIS 83 (Ky., Oct. 29, 2015)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
ON APPEAL FROM KENTON CIRCUIT COURT. HONORABLE GREGORYM. BARTLETT, JUDGE. NO. 87-CR-00166-001.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Kenton Circuit Court, Kentucky, denied defendant's motions to prohibit execution of the death sentence because of mental retardation (mental retardation motion) and to compel deoxyribonucleic acid (DNA) testing of hairs and semen found in the victim's automobile and used by the prosecution in his 1988 trial (DNA motion). Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: The Supreme Court of Kentucky held that the trial court erred in denying defendant's mental retardation motion under Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 532.130-140 without an evidentiary hearing. Defendant was entitled to an evidentiary hearing on his mental retardation motion because he produced some evidence that he was mentally retarded. He submitted school records indicating that at age 14, he had an IQ of 62, was reading at a third-grade level, and was performing work at a first- and second-grade level. The fact that there were also positive indications of a defendant's adaptive behavior went to the weight and credibility of the evidence. The court also held that (1) Kentucky's procedures for establishing whether capital offenders were mentally retarded did not violate defendant's due process rights; (2) the trial court properly denied a motion for DNA testing of hairs; (3) defendant was not entitled to an evidentiary hearing to determine if the hairs were destroyed after a preservation order; (4) the trial court erred by failing to rule on defendant's request for DNA testing of semen; and (5) defendant was not entitled to DNA testing under the federal or state constitutions.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the ruling denying defendant's motion for DNA testing of hair; vacated the trial court's order to the extent that it failed to rule on whether defendant was entitled to DNA testing of semen and remanded that issue for a ruling; and vacated the trial court's ruling on the mental retardation motion and remanded that issue for an evidentiary hearing.

Singleton v. Commonwealth


2010-SC-000078-DG


SUPREME COURT OF KENTUCKY


364 S.W.3d 97; 2012 Ky. LEXIS 39



April 26, 2012, Rendered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication May 17, 2012.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
ON REVIEW FROM COURT OF APPEALS. CASE NO. 2009-CA-000328-MR. CASEY CIRCUIT COURT NO. 08-CR-00082.
Commonwealth v. Singleton, 2010 Ky. App. Unpub. LEXIS 5 (Ky. Ct. App., Jan. 8, 2010)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A trial court granted defendant's motion to suppress evidence obtained from his vehicle during a stop at a traffic checkpoint. The Court of Appeals of Kentucky reversed, holding that the evidence was properly obtained. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: The court held that defendant's detention at the checkpoint unduly infringed on his Fourth Amendment right to be free from unreasonable seizures of his person and searches of his property, and therefore the evidence obtained as a result of his unconstitutional detention was properly suppressed by the trial court. The court held that a checkpoint for the purpose of general crime control or detection of ordinary criminal wrongdoing included violations of a city ordinance. The court further held that because the checkpoint's only purpose was to enforce a revenue-raising tax upon vehicles in the city and the checkpoint to enforce the sticker ordinance comported with none of the purposes that the United States Supreme Court had found to be important enough to override the individual liberty interests secured by the Fourth Amendment.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed and the order suppressing the evidence was reinstated.

 

81 of 1068 DOCUMENTS



Legislative Research Comm'n v. Fischer


2012-SC-000091-TG AND 2012-SC-000092-TG


SUPREME COURT OF KENTUCKY


366 S.W.3d 905; 2012 Ky. LEXIS 55



April 26, 2012, Rendered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication May 17, 2012.
Related proceeding at Brown v. Kentucky, 2013 U.S. Dist. LEXIS 90401 (E.D. Ky., June 27, 2013)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
ON APPEAL FROM FRANKLIN CIRCUIT COURT. HONORABLE PHILLIP J. SHEPHERD, JUDGE. NO. 12-CI-00109.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, the Kentucky Legislative Research Commission, sought review of a judgment from the Franklin Circuit Court (Kentucky), which, in a suit brought by appellee voters, found redistricting plans for the Kentucky House of Representatives and the Kentucky Senate to be unconstitutional and issued a temporary injunction prohibiting the implementation of the legislative districts created by the plans.


OVERVIEW: Each of the redistricting plans contained one district with a population deviation greater than 5 percent from the ideal district as determined by reference to census data. Alternative plans were proposed in both chambers that divided fewer counties and that had a population variance within plus-or-minus 5 percent of the ideal districts. The court held that the redistricting plans violated Ky. Const. § 33 by failing to divide the fewest number of counties possible and by failing to achieve sufficient population equality. Although it was not possible to avoid all splitting of counties without violating equal protection principles, county integrity had to be balanced with population equality to accommodate both. Population equality was not presumed where population deviations exceeded 5 percent. The existence of alternative conforming plans did not establish unconstitutionality in itself but showed that greater population inequality was not a necessary consequence of pursuing county integrity, and no other rational state policy had been advanced. The finding of unconstitutionality made the plans null and void; thus, even if the plans were more efficient, they could not be used.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment and remanded with directions to enjoin permanently the conduct of any election under the district boundaries established under the unconstitutional redistricting plans.

Hancock v. Prestonsburg Indus. Corp.


2010-SC-000376-DG


SUPREME COURT OF KENTUCKY


365 S.W.3d 199; 2012 Ky. LEXIS 35



April 26, 2012, Rendered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication May 17, 2012.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
ON REVIEW FROM COURT OF APPEALS. CASE NO. 2009-CA-001144-MR. FLOYD CIRCUIT COURT NO. 05-CI-00638.
Hancock v. Ky. Bd. of Tax Appeals & Prestonsburg Indus. Corp., 2010 Ky. App. LEXIS 86 (Ky. Ct. App., May 7, 2010)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Kentucky Court of Appeals concluded that appellee organization was a purely charitable organization and thus ad valorem exempt from taxation. The test applied by the appellate court for the exemption was whether appellee's activities reasonably bettered mankind. Discretionary review was accepted.


OVERVIEW: The court concluded that appellee's real property was not tax exempt under Ky. Const. § 170. The evidence did not establish that appellee was a purely public charity or that its property was employed for a purely charitable purpose. Appellee's activities were inconsistent with a "purely public charity" as its activities were not wholly altruistic in the end to be attained. Appellee's actual activities involved developing and marketing commercial property, activities which primarily served and benefited appellee's buyers or customers. There was no evidence that appellee's buyers/customers would qualify as persons or organizations in need of charity or general public assistance. Neither was appellee claiming to actually create jobs, but rather it claimed only the incidental benefit of bringing in new businesses, which might potentially create jobs.


OUTCOME: The opinion of the appellate court was reversed, and the matter was remanded to the circuit court.

Maynes v. Commonwealth


2010-SC-000681-DG


SUPREME COURT OF KENTUCKY


361 S.W.3d 922; 2012 Ky. LEXIS 20

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Kentucky Court of Appeals entered a judgment in a criminal case that upheld the trial court's order imposing court costs on defendant. The state supreme court granted defendant's motion for discretionary review of that decision.


OVERVIEW: Defendant, who was 19-years-old, was involved with others in a residential burglary. Because defendant's role appeared to be minor, he was offered a plea deal that diverted his sentence. He accepted the offer, which was approved subject to the condition that within six months, he pay statutory court costs of $ 130.00 to the trial court clerk. It determined that Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 23A.205 requiring convicted people to pay court costs allowed court costs to be waived for "poor persons," as defined in Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 453.190(2), but that defendant could reasonably be expected to work and pay the modest amount imposed because he had been released from custody.


OUTCOME: The state supreme court affirmed the appellate court's judgment.

TECO Mech. Contr., Inc. v. Commonwealth


2009-SC-000821-DG


SUPREME COURT OF KENTUCKY


366 S.W.3d 386; 2012 Ky. LEXIS 23; 19 Wage & Hour Cas. 2d (BNA) 565; 162 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P61,238

SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication April 12, 2012.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
ON REVIEW FROM COURT OF APPEALS. CASE NO. 2008-CA-000305-MR. FRANKLIN CIRCUIT COURT NO. 05-CI-00464.

DISPOSITION: AFFIRMING.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Franklin Circuit Court, Kentucky, found that Kentucky's prevailing wage law did not violate the Constitutions of either the Commonwealth or the United States. The Kentucky Court of Appeals affirmed. Appellant contractor appealed.


OVERVIEW: The contractor argued that Kentucky's prevailing wage law violated procedural due process because it permitted appellee Kentucky Labor Cabinet (Cabinet) imposed back wages and civil penalties and demanded that the contractor pay these amounts. The supreme court found that so long as the Cabinet followed the procedures set forth in the statutes, it could not deprive a contractor of property without court intervention. The Cabinet's actions did not deprive the contractor of a protected property interest. The contractor did not allege sufficient injury to meet the "plus" element of the stigma plus test. The Cabinet impaired the contractor's ability to conduct business with the prime contractors by impugning the contractor's reputation. It took no other action to alter the contractor's legal status or inhibit it from conducting future business with the prime contractors. There were sufficient safeguards to prevent the Cabinet from abusing any legislative or judicial authority granted to it under the prevailing wage law. The Cabinet could not exercise unfettered discretion, Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 337.505. The Cabinet did not engage in agency adjudication.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Dunn v. Commonwealth


2010-SC-000234-MR


SUPREME COURT OF KENTUCKY


360 S.W.3d 751; 2012 Ky. LEXIS 8



February 23, 2012, Rendered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication March 15, 2012.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
ON APPEAL FROM MONTGOMERY CIRCUIT COURT. HONORABLE WILLIAM EVANS LANE, JUDGE. CASE NO. 08-CR-00226.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Montgomery Circuit Court (Kentucky) that convicted him of five counts of first-degree sodomy involving a 14-year-old boy, arguing that the trial court erred in denying his motion to suppress a condom evidence because a condom found on his property was within the curtilage of his home and was discovered without a warrant.


OVERVIEW: The victim disclosed that he had been sodomized repeatedly by defendant, his aunt's boyfriend. When he told the police that he could show them the location in the woods where defendant assaulted him, the police took the boy to the location and discovered a condom that defendant had discarded. Both defendant's and the boy's DNA were detected on the condom. Defendant was charged with sodomy, and the trial court ruled that the condom was admissible because it was found in an outdoor area that was not part of the curtilage of defendant's home. On review, the court held that the area was not part of the curtilage of defendant's home and was not protected from warrantless search and seizure because the area was approximately 400 feet from the house, the area was not enclosed by a fence, and defendant used that portion of property for hunting and four-wheeling, which were not intimate activities of the home that would show that the area was part of the curtilage. Therefore, the search did not violate the Fourth Amendment, U.S. Const. amend. IV, or Ky. Const. § 10.


OUTCOME: The judgment of conviction was affirmed.

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Muhlenberg Circuit Court (Kentucky) convicting him of murder and sentencing him to life in prison.


OVERVIEW: The court held that the trial court did not err by excluding evidence concerning the nature of an alleged accomplice's prior crime because, to the extent defendant sought to impeach her, the evidence was inadmissible under Ky. The trial court did not err by refusing to give a missing evidence instruction because the detective's destruction of his notes once they had been incorporated in his report appeared to have been routine.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Bailey v. Pres. Rural Rds. of Madison County


2009-SC-000417-DG


SUPREME COURT OF KENTUCKY


394 S.W.3d 350; 2011 Ky. LEXIS 175



December 22, 2011, Rendered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication January 12, 2012.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
ON REVIEW FROM COURT OF APPEALS. CASE NO. 2008-CA-000696-MR, MADISON CIRCUIT COURT NO. 06-CI-00442.
Bailey v. Pres. Rural Rds. of Madison County, Inc., 2009 Ky. App. Unpub. LEXIS 272 (Ky. Ct. App., June 5, 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellees, a county resident and a nonprofit association, filed an action challenging a decision of the fiscal court to discontinue maintenance on a county road under Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 178.070, and against appellant property owner, who erected a locked gate blocking the road. The circuit court enjoined the property owner from blocking the road with a gate, and the Court of Appeals (Kentucky) affirmed. The property owner appealed.


OVERVIEW: The county resident did not live or own property along the road. The nonprofit consisted of various residents, including one member who owed property exclusively accessed by the road. They wanted the road to remain open and unobstructed because it provided them with a shortcut to other destinations and provided access to several sites on the road that were important to their families' history. While the individual resident failed to who any specific injury sufficient to provide standing, with the member who owned property on the road, the nonprofit had associational standing to assert the claim. Summary judgment holding that the fiscal court properly discontinued maintenance of the road pursuant to § 178.070 was not appealed and, therefore, became a final adjudication on that point. Thus, the road was discontinued as a county-maintained way under § 178.070. Further, no petition satisfying the requirements of Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 178.116(4) was ever filed and, thus, the road had to remain an open, public road. Finally, there was no unconstitutional taking of property and there was no constitutional infirmity to the applications of Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. §§ 178.070 and -.116.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

Vision Mining, Inc. v. Gardner


2010-SC-000311-WC AND 2010-SC-000438-WC


SUPREME COURT OF KENTUCKY


364 S.W.3d 455; 2011 Ky. LEXIS 177



December 22, 2011, Rendered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication May 24, 2012.
Rehearing denied by Vision Mining, Inc. v. Gardner, 2012 Ky. LEXIS 445 (Ky., May 24, 2012)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
ON REVIEW FROM COURT OF APPEALS. CASE NO. 2009-CA-000874-WC. WORKERS' COMPENSATION BOARD NO. 07-WC-01156.

ON REVIEW FROM COURT OF APPEALS. CASE NO. 2009-CA-000927-WC. WORKERS' COMPENSATION BOARD NO. 02-WC-01692.
Gardner v. Vision Mining, Inc., 2010 Ky. App. LEXIS 69 (Ky. Ct. App., Apr. 9, 2010)
Martinez v. Peabody Coal Co., 2010 Ky. App. LEXIS 102 (Ky. Ct. App., June 4, 2010)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellee coal workers sought compensation benefits for coal workers' pneumoconiosis (black lung). The Workers' Compensation Board affirmed decisions to dismiss both cases because the consensus readings of their X-rays found them negative for black lung. On review, however, the Court of Appeals (Kentucky) held that the consensus procedure required by Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 342.316 was unconstitutional. Appellant employers sought review.


OVERVIEW: For coal workers' pneumoconiosis, Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 342.316(3) required a different procedure to establish its existence than it required for all other types of pneumoconiosis. In addition, § 342.316(13) required clear and convincing evidence to rebut a panel consensus for coal workers' pneumoconiosis claims, while Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 342.315(2), addressing other occupational pneumoconiosis and diseases, required only a reasonable basis to rebut a university evaluator's clinical findings and opinions. There was no rational basis or substantial and justifiable reason for the disparate treatment of coal workers in this instance. Pneumoconiosis caused by exposure to coal dust was the same disease as pneumoconiosis caused by exposure to dust particles in other industries, yet coal workers faced different, higher standard-of-proof requirements than those other workers. Consequently, the consensus procedure required by Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 342.316 for proving the existence of coal workers' pneumoconiosis, and the clear and convincing standard the statute required to rebut such a consensus, violated the equal protection guarantees of U.S. Const. amend. XIV and Ky. Const. §§ 1-3.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

Cook v. Popplewell


2009-SC-000341-DG


SUPREME COURT OF KENTUCKY


394 S.W.3d 323; 2011 Ky. LEXIS 174; 33 I.E.R. Cas. (BNA) 1377



December 22, 2011, Rendered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication January 12, 2012.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
ON REVIEW FROM COURT OF APPEALS. CASE NO. 2008-CA-001249-MR. RUSSELL CIRCUIT COURT NO. 06-CI-00275.
Cook v. Popplewell, 2009 Ky. App. LEXIS 64 (Ky. Ct. App., May 15, 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant candidate, a deputy clerk, was discharged from her position shortly after announcing her candidacy for the office of county clerk. Following her defeat in the primary election, the candidate brought an action against appellees, the incumbent county clerk and the county, under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983. The circuit court dismissed the complaint by summary judgment, and the Court of Appeals (Kentucky) affirmed. The candidate appealed.


OVERVIEW: First, the county clerk and the county were not immune from the § 1983 claim because subdivisions of the State were "persons" for the purposes of § 1983 and were not entitled to be shielded from liability by State-created immunities. On the merits of the claim, the candidate alleged that she had been discharged from her position as deputy clerk in violation of her rights under U.S. Const. amends. I and XIV. The county clerk maintained that she was not aware of the candidate's intention to run against her and that the discharge was for work-related reasons. However, the resolution of that factual point was not germane to the appeal because, upon review of the dismissal by summary judgment, the facts were viewed from the candidate's perspective. Settled law held that there was no U.S. Const. amend. I right to candidacy, and there was no reason to deviate from settled law on the point. The First Amendment afforded no constitutional protection to candidacy for political office per se. Accordingly, the candidate failed to state a claim under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983. Finally, the candidate's cursory U.S. Const. amend. XIV due process argument did not comply with Ky. R. Civ. P. 76.12(4)(c)(v).


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment.

Univ. Med. Ctr., Inc. v. Beglin


2009-SC-000289-DG, 2009-SC-000839-DG


SUPREME COURT OF KENTUCKY


375 S.W.3d 783; 2011 Ky. LEXIS 162



October 27, 2011, Rendered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication March 22, 2012.
Rehearing denied by Univ. Med. Ctr., Inc. v. Beglin, 2012 Ky. LEXIS 572 (Ky., Mar. 22, 2012)
Appeal after remand at Univ. Med. Ctr., Inc. v. Beglin, 2014 Ky. App. LEXIS 67 (Ky. Ct. App., Apr. 25, 2014)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
ON REVIEW FROM COURT OF APPEALS. CASE NOS. 2007-CA-000018-MR AND 2007-CA-000133-MR. JEFFERSON CIRCUIT COURT NO. 04-CI-001605.
Univ. Med. Ctr., Inc. v. Beglin, 2009 Ky. App. Unpub. LEXIS 361 (Ky. Ct. App., Jan. 16, 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a medical malpractice/wrongful death action, defendant medical center challenged an opinion of the Kentucky Court of Appeals that affirmed a judgment in favor of plaintiffs, arguing that the trial court erred in instructing the jury on the adverse inference that could be made on the basis of missing evidence and in instructing the jury on punitive damages.


OVERVIEW: During surgery, the patient suffered an unexpected, substantial blood loss. Because of a delay in obtaining blood for transfusion, the patient sustained an anoxic brain injury, and she succumbed after being removed from a life support system. Following the surgery, a nurse prepared an incident report, which the medical center was unable to produce during discovery in the ensuing lawsuit. At trial, the trial court instructed the jury that it could infer that the missing incident report would have been adverse to the medical center. On review, the court held that, because the incident report was within the exclusive control of the medical center and its loss was inexplicable, the jury was properly instructed that it could find that the evidence was intentionally and in bad faith destroyed or concealed and that, if available, its contents would be adverse to the medical center. However, the trial court erred in instructing the jury on punitive damages because such an instruction was only proper if the evidence supported a finding that the medical center should have anticipated the inordinate delay in obtaining blood for transfusion or that it authorized or ratified the delay.


OUTCOME: The opinion of the court of appeals was affirmed in part and reversed in part, and the case was remanded to the trial court for entry of a new judgment that excluded the punitive damages award.

Chavies v. Commonwealth


2010-SC-000479-MR


SUPREME COURT OF KENTUCKY


354 S.W.3d 103; 2011 Ky. LEXIS 161



October 27, 2011, Rendered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication October 27, 2011.
Post-conviction relief dismissed at Chavies v. Commonwealth, 2015 Ky. App. Unpub. LEXIS 544 (Ky. Ct. App., July 31, 2015)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
ON APPEAL FROM LAUREL CIRCUIT COURT. HONORABLE JOHN KNOX MILLS, JUDGE. NO. 09-CR-00278-001.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Laurel Circuit Court (Kentucky) convicting him of manufacturing methamphetamine, receiving of stolen property worth $500 or more, but less than $10,000, and being a second-degree persistent felony offender, and sentencing him to 50 years' imprisonment.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, the court held that the trial court did not err by denying defendant's motion to suppress evidence seized in the search of his vehicle because: (1) the traffic stop was constitutional, as the officer stopped defendant for not wearing a seatbelt and reckless driving; (2) the police saw a laptop bag and lights in plain view inside the vehicle, which they had probable cause to believe were recently stolen; and (3) defendant's vehicle was readily mobile even though defendant was under arrest at the time the officers conducted the search. The trial court did not err by denying a directed verdict for manufacturing methamphetamine under Ky. Rev. Stat. § 218A.1432(1)(b) because a detective testified that: (1) he found four plastic bottles at the scene, three that were inactive methamphetamine labs and on that was an HCL generator used to cook methamphetamine; (2) three of the bottles tested positive for ammonia; (3) he also found rock salt, plastic tubing, a measuring cup, a spoon, a funnel, and lighter fluid in the vehicle, all of which were commonly used in cooking methamphetamine.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

92 of 1068 DOCUMENTS


Shawnee Telecom Res., Inc. v. Brown


2009-SC-000574-DG


SUPREME COURT OF KENTUCKY


354 S.W.3d 542; 2011 Ky. LEXIS 159



October 27, 2011, Rendered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication October 27, 2011.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
ON REVIEW FROM COURT OF APPEALS. CASE NO. 2008-CA-000042-MR AND 2008-CA-000167-MR. FAYETTE CIRCUIT COURT NO. 03-CI-05040.
Shawnee Telecom Res., Inc. v. Brown, 2009 Ky. App. Unpub. LEXIS 675 (Ky. Ct. App., Aug. 14, 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant corporation sought review of a judgment from the Court of Appeals (Kentucky), which vacated the trial court's judgment appraising appellee dissenter's shares pursuant to Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 271B.13-300 following a cash-out merger.


OVERVIEW: The trial court adopted a commissioner's report that discounted the capitalized earnings value for lack of marketability, considered the net asset value, and averaged the two values. The court found no error in the trial court's Ky. R. Civ. P. 54.02 ruling severing the appraisal from other claims against the dissenter because the valuation judgment finally determined a statutory claim that was distinct and severable. An offhand remark by the trial judge indicating that he expected to be accused of bias did not reasonably call his impartiality into question under Ky. Sup. Ct. R. 4.300 and thus did not require recusal. The court agreed with the court of appeals that marketability discounts were inappropriate at the shareholder level in determining fair value as defined in Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 271B.13-010(3) and that the Delaware block method of appraisal was not required. The court overruled Ford v. Courier-Journal Job Printing Co., 639 S.W.2d 553 (Ky. App. 1982) to the extent inconsistent. Net asset valuation methods, which involved estimating the market value of company assets, were not precluded by the fair value standard. Entity-level discounts were permissible.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the court of appeals' conclusion that the appraisal order had to be vacated, reversed the court of appeals' rejection of the net asset approach, and remanded to the trial court for reappraisal.

Greene v. Commonwealth


2007-SC-000511-DG, 2008-SC-000783-DG


SUPREME COURT OF KENTUCKY


349 S.W.3d 892; 2011 Ky. LEXIS 128



September 22, 2011, Rendered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication October 13, 2011


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
ON REVIEW FROM COURT OF APPEALS. CASE NO. 2006-CA-000683-MR. FRANKLIN CIRCUIT COURT NO. 04-CI-00353. ON REVIEW FROM COURT OF APPEALS. CASE NO. 2006-CA-000683-MR. FRANKLIN CIRCUIT COURT NO. 04-CI-00353.
Commonwealth v. Greene, 2008 Ky. LEXIS 309 (Ky., Dec. 10, 2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Heirs who were entitled to receive, and never did receive, the net proceeds of a judicial sale of land brought claims against the former circuit court master commissioner and others in the Board of Claims (Board) under the Kentucky Board of Claims Act, Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. §§ 44.070, et. seq. The Board dismissed for lack of jurisdiction. The circuit court and the Court of Appeals (Kentucky) affirmed. The supreme court granted discretionary review.


OVERVIEW: The Court of Appeals erred when it held that the Board of Claims lacked jurisdiction over the defendant circuit court judge in his official capacity. The judge was an officer of the Commonwealth pursuant to Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 61.020. The Commonwealth waived its sovereign immunity, to the extent provided in the Board of Claims Act, for claims based upon allegations of negligence by a circuit court judge in the performance of ministerial functions of his office under Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 44.073(2) and the judge's continued use of a master commissioner, without reappointment, to perform significant functions in actions in the circuit court without a bond, and without surety was grounds for a claim in the Board of Claims based upon alleged negligence in the performance of a ministerial duty by an officer of the state.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the Court of Appeals was reversed and the case was remanded to the Board of Claims for further proceedings.

 

94 of 1068 DOCUMENTS


Turpin v. Commonwealth


2010-SC-000550-MR


SUPREME COURT OF KENTUCKY


350 S.W.3d 444; 2011 Ky. LEXIS 130



September 22, 2011, Rendered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication October 13, 2011


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
ON APPEAL FROM CASEY CIRCUIT COURT. HONORABLE JULIA HYLTON ADAMS, SPECIAL JUDGE. NO. 09-CR-00076.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted in the Casey Circuit Court (Kentucky) of possession of a firearm by a convicted felon, in violation of Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 527.040. Defendant was also found to be a first-degree persistent felony offender and was sentenced to a maximum term of twenty years in prison. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued, inter alia, that the twenty-year sentence imposed when he was found to be a persistent felony offender (PFO) was unconstitutionally excessive in violation of Ky. Const. § 17 and U.S. Const. amend. VIII, given the remoteness of two of his prior offenses, the relatively minor nature of all of his offenses, and the Commonwealth's own recognition, as evidenced by its plea offer, that a shorter sentence was appropriate. The supreme court disagreed. The twenty-year recidivist sentence with the possibility of parole in no more than four years, while harsh, was simply not an extreme sentence; it did not approach the upper limits of Kentucky's sentencing structure and could not be deemed "grossly disproportionate" as the Supreme Court has employed that term. As defendant's sentence was neither extreme within Kentucky's sentencing scheme nor grossly disproportionate for a third felony, however "soft" those felonies may have been, he was not entitled to relief.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Meece v. Commonwealth


2006-SC-000881-MR


SUPREME COURT OF KENTUCKY


348 S.W.3d 627; 2011 Ky. LEXIS 93



June 16, 2011, Rendered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication October 27, 2011.
Rehearing denied by Meece v. Commonwealth, 2011 Ky. LEXIS 259 (Ky., Oct. 27, 2011)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Meece v. Kentucky, 568 U.S. 828, 133 S. Ct. 105, 184 L. Ed. 2d 49, 2012 U.S. LEXIS 6812 (Oct. 1, 2012)
Post-conviction relief denied at Meece v. Commonwealth, 2017 Ky. LEXIS 423 (Ky., Sept. 28, 2017)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
ON APPEAL FROM WARREN CIRCUIT COURT. HONORABLE JAMES G. WEDDLE, SPECIAL JUDGE. NO. 06-CR-00656.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Warren Circuit Court (Kentucky) sentencing him to consecutive 24-year terms of confinement on two convictions of robbery and burglary and to death for each of three murder convictions, arguing that the trial court erred in allowing the Commonwealth to introduce his statements into evidence and erred in allowing the Commonwealth to introduce evidence in violation of Ky. R. Evid. 404(b).


OVERVIEW: Defendant shot and killed the victims, a husband and wife and their son, at the behest and with the assistance of their daughter during a robbery and burglary. Defendant initially entered into a plea agreement with the State but subsequently withdrew his plea and proceeded to trial. On review, the court held that, while Ky. R. Evid. 410 precluded the Commonwealth from introducing into evidence statements made by defendant during plea negotiations, defendant's two statements were admissible because they were made after the formal plea agreement was fully negotiated and signed. Further, the trial court did not err under Ky. R. Evid. 404(b) in allowing a witness to testify that defendant had a human silhouette target hanging outside his cubicle at work with approximately 26 bullet holes in an upper body spray pattern because the evidence was relevant to establish defendant's ability, knowledge, and competency with firearms. The Commonwealth had the right to show defendant's particular way of firing at targets representing human beings and his skill in doing so; although the evidence was prejudicial to defendant, it was not unfairly prejudicial.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the circuit court was affirmed.

 

96 of 1068 DOCUMENTS


Bennington v. Commonwealth


2009-SC-000521-MR


SUPREME COURT OF KENTUCKY


348 S.W.3d 613; 2011 Ky. LEXIS 81



May 19, 2011, Rendered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication October 27, 2011.
Rehearing denied by Bennington v. Commonwealth, 2011 Ky. LEXIS 283 (Ky., Oct. 27, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
ON APPEAL FROM BULLITT CIRCUIT COURT. HONORABLE RODNEY D. BURRESS, JUDGE. NO. 07-CR-00120.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Bullitt Circuit Court (Kentucky) that convicted him of multiple sex crimes against his daughter that occurred over the course of 15 years and began when she was merely six years old, arguing that the evidence was insufficient to support his convictions.


OVERVIEW: On review, the court held that the evidence was sufficient to support defendant's sodomy convictions. Count 4 charged defendant with engaging in oral sex with his daughter when she was 14 years old, and Count 5 charged him with doing so when the girl was 15 years old. The victim testified that, when she was 14, oral sex occurred "all the time" and that, when she was 15, sexual activity--both vaginal and oral--occurred daily. As such, the evidence was sufficient to convict defendant of those crimes. Further, such testimony was sufficient to support defendant's conviction for raping his daughter when she was 15. However the evidence was insufficient to support defendant's conviction for raping the girl when she was 14 because there was no testimony from the victim that defendant had begun engaging her in sexual intercourse by that point. Further, the evidence was sufficient to support defendant's six incest convictions because the victim testified that, from the time she turned 15 until she left home at age 22, sexual intercourse occurred daily.


OUTCOME: Defendant's conviction of one count of first-degree rape was reversed for insufficient evidence, but defendant's remaining convictions of four counts of sodomy, one count of first-degree sexual abuse, three counts of third-degree rape, and six counts of incest were affirmed.

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, David Lee Sanders, appeals from an order of the Madison Circuit Court (Kentucky) denying his petition for postconviction relief pursuant to Ky. R. Civ. P. 60.02, arguing that the appointment of a special judge to preside over his postconviction proceeding was unconstitutional and that the his postconviction counsel failed to render effective assistance.

Nash v. Campbell County Fiscal Court


2009-SC-000152-DG


SUPREME COURT OF KENTUCKY


345 S.W.3d 811; 2011 Ky. LEXIS 57



April 21, 2011, Rendered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication August 25, 2011.
Rehearing denied by Nash v. Campbell County Fiscal Court, 2011 Ky. LEXIS 523 (Ky., Aug. 25, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
ON REVIEW FROM COURT OF APPEALS. CASE NOS. 2007-CA-000994-MR & 2007-CA-001065-MR. CAMPBELL CIRCUIT COURT NOS. 05-CI-00886 & 05-CI-01254.
Campbell County Fiscal Court v. Nash, 2008 Ky. App. LEXIS 373 (Ky. Ct. App., Dec. 12, 2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant owners challenged a decision from the Kentucky Court of Appeals, which reversed a trial court's decision that two ordinances were unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: A dispute arose after a subdivision of property was sought by the owners. The trial court found that two ordinances were unconstitutional, but the court of appeals reversed. This appeal followed. In partially affirming, the supreme court determined that the agricultural supremacy clause took agricultural uses outside of the jurisdiction of zoning ordinances and agricultural divisions outside of the jurisdiction of subdivision regulations. The issue here was whether the proposed conveyances had to have planning commission approval or whether they were exempt under Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. §§ 100.111(22), 100.111(2), and 100.203(4). Three elements had to exist for an exemption. The conveyances here did not meet the requirement that the use was restricted to agricultural uses. Moreover, the conveyances did not meet the third requirement, which was that all parcels had to have frontage on an existing street.


OUTCOME: The portion of the court of appeals' decision holding two county ordinances valid was reversed. The decision to remand the case to the trial court for the entry of summary judgment was affirmed. The case was remanded to the trial court with instructions to make an entry of summary judgment.

Nelson County Bd. of Educ. v. Forte


2009-SC-000715-DG, 2010-SC-000149-DG


SUPREME COURT OF KENTUCKY


337 S.W.3d 617; 2011 Ky. LEXIS 53



April 21, 2011, Rendered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication May 12, 2011.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
ON REVIEW FROM COURT OF APPEALS. CASE NO. 2008-CA-001958-MR. NELSON CIRCUIT COURT NO. 08-CI-00625. ON REVIEW FROM COURT OF APPEALS. CASE NO. 2009-CA-001635-MR. NELSON CIRCUIT COURT NOS. 07-CI-00164 AND 07-CI-00338.
Nelson County Bd. of Educ. v. Forte, 2009 Ky. App. LEXIS 192 (Ky. Ct. App., Oct. 9, 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court granted review of consolidated appeals from decisions of the Kentucky Court of Appeals. One matter was a wrongful death tort action filed in civil court by appellant widower against appellee school district after his wife, a school teacher, died on school grounds. The second case was a claim filed at the Kentucky Board of Claims, based on the same facts, in which appellant was a board of education and the widower was appellee.


OVERVIEW: In the Board of Claims action, the court of appeals held that the action was time-barred. The court disagreed with that conclusion, finding that Ky. Rev. Stat. § 403.270 gave the widower 90 days following the conclusion of the action in the circuit court to file his Board of Claims action. It determined, however, that the Board of Claims action was premature and a nullity, since the same cause of action was then properly pending before the circuit court. In the tort action, the court of appeals dismissed the widower's appeal of the summary judgment against him, for reasons of governmental immunity, due to his failure to name an indispensable party in his notice of appeal. Joinder rules were not dispositive, so the court looked at Ky. R. Civ. P. 73.03, requiring that an appellant name all appellees. The court rejected the widower's argument that any relief against the school district would also cover the board of education and thus the board of education was not indispensable to the appeal. The board of education was an indispensable party on appeal and the widower's notice of appeal was therefore jurisdictionally defective.


OUTCOME: In the appeal of the Board of Claims action, the court reversed the court of appeals on its ultimate conclusion and ordered that the widower be allowed to refile his action in the future. In the direct appeal of the underlying tort action, the court affirmed the decision of the court of appeals.

Morgan v. Botts


2009-SC-000515-TG, 2009-SC-000751-TG, 2009-SC-000818-TG


SUPREME COURT OF KENTUCKY


348 S.W.3d 599; 2011 Ky. LEXIS 55



April 21, 2011, Rendered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication October 27, 2011.
Rehearing denied by, Petition granted by Morgan & Pottinger, Attys., P.S.C. v. Botts, 2011 Ky. LEXIS 277 (Ky., Oct. 27, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
ON APPEAL FROM MERCER CIRCUIT COURT. HONORABLE DARREN PECKLER, JUDGE. NO. 07-CI-00224. ON APPEAL FROM MERCER CIRCUIT COURT. HONORABLE DARREN PECKLER, JUDGE. NO. 07-CI-00224.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants former client and its new attorneys (Client) filed a disciplinary complaint against plaintiff former attorney (Attorney). The disciplinary charges were ultimately dismissed. Attorney sued client for wrongful use of civil proceedings, defamation and slander, abuse of process, fraud, and outrageous conduct. The Mercer Circuit Court (Kentucky) denied Client's motion to dismiss. Client appealed.


OVERVIEW: Client argued, inter alia, that they were immune from suit pursuant to the judicial statements privilege and Ky. Sup. Ct. R. 3.160(4). The supreme court agreed. Any statement made preliminary to, in the institution of, or during the course of an attorney disciplinary proceeding was be privileged so long as it was material, pertinent, and relevant to such proceeding. This included statements contained in the ethics complaint. In a matter of first impression, the supreme court concluded that the judicial statements privilege encompassed the act of filing the complaint, in addition to the statements contained therein. Any communication or statement made to the Kentucky Bar Association during the course of a disciplinary hearing or investigation, including the contents of the bar complaint initiating such proceedings, were absolutely privileged. This privilege extended to any claim relating to the act of filing the bar complaint, such as abuse of process, wrongful use of civil proceedings, or malicious prosecution.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed and the case was remanded.

Harris v. Commonwealth


2009-SC-000621-MR


SUPREME COURT OF KENTUCKY


338 S.W.3d 222; 2011 Ky. LEXIS 42



March 24, 2011, Rendered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication June 16, 2011.
Rehearing denied by Harris v. Commonwealth, 2011 Ky. LEXIS 611 (Ky., June 16, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
ON APPEAL FROM JEFFERSON CIRCUIT COURT. HONORABLE GEOFFREY P. MORRIS, JUDGE. NOS. 07-CR-003307 AND 09-CR-000005.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a decision of the Jefferson Circuit Court (Kentucky), which convicted him of numerous charges including a second-degree persistent felony offender enhancement. In accordance with the jury's recommendation, defendant was sentenced to a total of 30 years' imprisonment for the crimes.


OVERVIEW: Defendant contended that the judgment rendered against him was void because a retired senior judge presided over his trial. the trial judge's service on the case as a special judge was by assignment of the chief justice, a process which defendant argued violated his right under the Kentucky Constitution to be tried by a duly-elected or a gubernatorially-appointed circuit court judge. After indictment, the case was assigned to the trial judge, who was then duly-elected judge. He later retired from his seat on the bench and entered into the senior judge program. Because of impending changes in the senior judge program, some 22 other judges across Kentucky also retired. With additional vacancies created by other causes, a total of 27 judgeships were vacant, which was an exceptionally high number. The court upheld the decision. At the time of his assignment as a special judge, the trial judge was a retired judge qualified for service in the vacant judgeship. The Kentucky Constitution gave the chief justice the authority to have made the assignment as he deemed necessary. As such, the trial judge was constitutionally empowered to preside over the case under Ky. Const. § 110(5)(b).


OUTCOME: The decision was affirmed.

Blackstone Mining Co. v. Travelers Ins. Co.


2009-SC-000015-DG


SUPREME COURT OF KENTUCKY


2010 Ky. LEXIS 283



December 16, 2010, Rendered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication December 16, 2010.
Modified by, Substituted opinion at, Remanded by Blackstone Mining Co. v. Travelers Ins. Co., 351 S.W.3d 193, 2010 Ky. LEXIS 320 (Ky., 2010)
Modified and rehearing denied by Blackstone Mining Co. v. Travelers Ins. Co., 2011 Ky. LEXIS 186 (Ky., Nov. 23, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [*1] 
ON REVIEW FROM COURT OF APPEALS. CASE NO. 2007-CA-001610-MR. PIKE CIRCUIT COURT NO. 97-CI-00684.
Travelers Ins. Co. v. Blackstone Mining Co., 2008 Ky. App. Unpub. LEXIS 12 (Ky. Ct. App., Oct. 17, 2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff insurer sued defendant mining company (company) alleging that the company had underpaid premiums under two workers' compensation policies. The company counterclaimed, alleging that it had overpaid the premiums and was entitled to a refund. The circuit court granted the company's motion for summary judgment. The Court of Appeals (Kentucky) reversed. The supreme court granted discretionary review.


OVERVIEW: The company argued, inter alia, that in light of the notices of 23 employees rejecting workers' compensation coverage and lowering premiums, the burden shifted to the insurer to produce affirmative evidence demonstrating that the rejections were not made voluntarily, and that the insurer failed to do so. The supreme court agreed. The court of appeals failed to credit the presumptive validity of the rejection notices. It was undisputed that 23 employees executed proper forms to reject workers' compensation coverage, and that the forms were properly filed and were presumptively valid. Through the signed rejection forms, the company met its initial burden of showing that no genuine issue of material fact existed on the issue of whether the employees had voluntarily rejected workers' compensation coverage. It was then incumbent upon the insurer to come forward with some evidence casting doubt on the validity of the notices. The insurer alleged, inter alia, that the employees were designated as officers of the company solely to qualify different coverage to reduce its workers' compensation premiums. Such allegations did not amount to affirmative evidence raising a genuine issue of fact.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed.

Blackstone Mining Co. v. Travelers Ins. Co.

Hill v. Petrotech Res. Corp.


2010-SC-000182-1


SUPREME COURT OF KENTUCKY


325 S.W.3d 302; 2010 Ky. LEXIS 254; 38 Media L. Rep. 2580



October 21, 2010, Rendered


NOTICE: TO BE PUBLISHED.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
ON REVIEW FROM COURT OF APPEALS. CASE NO. 2009-CA-002244-1. BARREN CIRCUIT COURT NO. 09-CI-00623.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff oil and gas drilling company (drilling company) sued defendant collection company (collection company) for defamation, invasion of privacy, and injunctive relief. The circuit court enjoined collection company from issuing public comments pertaining to drilling company or its business dealings. The Court of Appeals (Kentucky) denied interlocutory relief. Collection company petitioned for interlocutory relief before the supreme court.


OVERVIEW: Collection company argued, inter alia, that the temporary injunction entered by the circuit court was an improper prior restraint of speech under the First Amendment of the United States Constitution, U.S. Const. amend. I. The supreme court agreed. The temporary injunction that prohibited collection company from making future statements about oil company was a prior restraint on speech. Defamatory speech could be enjoined only after the trial court's final determination by a preponderance of the evidence that the speech at issue was, in fact, false, and only then upon the condition that the injunction was narrowly tailored to limit the prohibited speech to that which had been judicially determined to be false. Application of the modern rule that defamatory speech could be enjoined only following a final determination that the speech was false compelled the result that collection company be granted the relief requested in his Ky. R. Civ. P. 65.09 motion, and that the injunction entered by the circuit court be set aside.


OUTCOME: The temporary injunction issued by the circuit court was dissolved.

Ky. PSC v. Commonwealth ex rel. Conway


2009-SC-000134-DG, 2009-SC-000150-DG


SUPREME COURT OF KENTUCKY


324 S.W.3d 373; 2010 Ky. LEXIS 257



October 21, 2010, Rendered

PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
ON REVIEW FROM COURT OF APPEALS. CASE NO. 2007-CA-001635-MR. FRANKLIN CIRCUIT COURT NOS. 02-CI-00499, 02-CI-01628, 03-CI-01189, 04-CI-01308, AND 06-CI-00269.
Ky. PSC v. Commonwealth ex rel. Stumbo, 2008 Ky. App. LEXIS 348 (Ky. Ct. App., Nov. 7, 2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Court of Appeals (Kentucky) affirmed trial court orders invalidating appellant utility's surcharge or rider aimed at recovering costs associated with the utility's program to accelerate improvement of its gas distribution mains as it existed before the enactment of Ky. Rev. Stat. § 278.509 in 2005, but reversed trial court orders invalidating the rider after the enactment of § 278.509. The court granted discretionary review.


OVERVIEW: The instant case involved five consolidated appeals by appellee Commonwealth from appellant Kentucky Public Service Commission's (PSC) orders over a five-year period approving and implementing a portion of the utility's rate schedule known as the accelerated main replacement program (AMRP) rider. On discretionary review, the court considered whether PSC had the plenary authority to allow the utility to adjust its rates by imposing the surcharge or rider. The court reversed in part and held that so long as the rates established by the utility were fair, just, and reasonable, the PSC had broad ratemaking power to allow recovery of such costs outside the parameters of a general rate case and even in the absence of a statute specifically authorizing recovery of such costs. The PSC had the plenary authority to regulate and investigate utilities and to ensure that rates charged were fair, just, and reasonable under Ky. Rev. Stat. §§ 278.030 and 278.040. The plenary powers were sufficient to permit PCS to approve the AMRP rider even before the enactment of Ky. Rev. Stat. § 278.509, which was merely a legislative codification and approval of the lawful actions the PCS had already taken.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the portion of the court of appeals' opinion that upheld the trial court's reversal of PSC orders predating the enactment of Ky. Rev. Stat. § 278.509 and affirmed, on other grounds, the portion of the court of appeals' opinion reinstating the PSC orders following the enactment of § 278.509. The court remanded to the trial court with directions to reinstate the PSC orders allowing for the rider or surcharge.

 

107 of 1068 DOCUMENTS


Cromwell Louisville Assocs., L.P. v. Commonwealth


2008-SC-000644-DG


SUPREME COURT OF KENTUCKY


323 S.W.3d 1; 2010 Ky. LEXIS 230



September 23, 2010, Rendered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication October 14, 2010.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
ON REVIEW FROM COURT OF APPEALS, CASE NO. 2007-CA-001128-MR. JEFFERSON CIRCUIT COURT NO. 05-CI-002545.
Commonwealth v. Cromwell Louisville Assocs., 2008 Ky. App. LEXIS 250 (Ky. Ct. App., Aug. 8, 2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayer challenged a decision from the Kentucky Court of Appeals, which reversed a trial court's decision to reverse a decision from the Kentucky Board of Tax Appeals. The Board of Tax appeals dismissed the taxpayer's challenge to appellee administrator's 2001 property value assessment as untimely under Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 133.120.


OVERVIEW: The Board of Tax Appeals found that Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 133.120 required the taxpayer to challenge a 2001 assessment during the 2001 inspection period. The trial court reversed the decision from the Board of Tax Appeals, and the Court of Appeals then reversed the trial court. This appeal followed. In affirming the Court of Appeals, the supreme court determined that the plain meaning of § 133.120, with its reference to the inspection period, mandated that the taxpayer request a conference during the current tax year. Construing the statute's time frame for the conference to a period of days without a limitation to the current tax year would have produced an absurd result. Further, the statute of limitations set forth in Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 134.590 was inapplicable. The taxpayer's due process deprivation claim was disingenuous. The taxpayer had to comply with the statutory language in Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 134.590, which required the exhaustion of administrative remedies before applying for a refund. The taxpayer did not do so in this case because there was a failure to request the assessment conference under Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 133.120(1)(a) during the same tax year.


OUTCOME: The decision of the Court of Appeals was affirmed. The case was remanded to the trial court with instructions to dismiss the action in accordance with the decision from the Board of Tax Appeals.

Windsor v. Commonwealth


2008-SC-000383-MR


SUPREME COURT OF KENTUCKY


2010 Ky. LEXIS 210



August 26, 2010, Rendered


NOTICE: TO BE PUBLISHED


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected September 20, 2010.
Substituted opinion at, Corrected by Windsor v. Commonwealth, 2010 Ky. LEXIS 312 (Ky., Aug. 26, 2010)
Modified by, Rehearing denied by Windsor v. Commonwealth, 2011 Ky. LEXIS 99 (Ky., Mar. 24, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [*1] 
ON APPEAL FROM JEFFERSON CIRCUIT COURT, HONORABLE MARTIN F. MCDONALD, JUDGE, NO. 04-CR-000001.
Windsor v. Commonwealth, 250 S.W.3d 306, 2008 Ky. LEXIS 105 (Ky., 2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Jefferson Circuit Court (Kentucky) that imposed two death sentences for the murder of his wife and son, arguing that the trial court erred in failing to revisit the issue of competency and in failing to find beyond a reasonable doubt that death was the appropriate punishment.


OVERVIEW: Defendant murdered his wife and their 8-year-old son by stabbing them with a kitchen knife and beating them with a dumbbell. The day trial was to commence, defendant took an overdose of prescription medication, which led to a competency hearing. Defendant was adjudicated competent, but before trial could proceed, defendant offered to plead guilty and accept a death sentence. At sentencing, the trial court found two statutory aggravating factors and sentenced defendant to death. On review, the court held that the trial court did not err in failing to revisit the issue of competency after defendant offered to plead guilty because the desire to plead guilty and accept the death penalty did not, by itself, create reasonable grounds within the meaning of Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 504.100(1), to question defendant's competency where no evidence demonstrated that defendant's mental health had deteriorated since the earlier competency hearing. Further, the trial court properly imposed a death penalty after finding statutory aggravating factors to exist; the trial court was not required to find beyond a reasonable doubt that death was the appropriate punishment.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

 

109 of 1068 DOCUMENTS


Louisville Metro Health Dep't v. Highview Manor Ass'n, LLC


2008-SC-000599-DG


SUPREME COURT OF KENTUCKY


319 S.W.3d 380; 2010 Ky. LEXIS 216



August 26, 2010, Rendered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication September 16, 2010.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
ON REVIEW FROM COURT OF APPEALS. CASE NO. 2007-CA-000233-DG. JEFFERSON CIRCUIT COURT NO. 06-XX-00021, JEFFERSON DISTRICT COURT NO. 06-C-00563.
Highview Manor Ass'n, LLC v. Louisville Metro Health Dep't, 2008 Ky. App. LEXIS 185 (Ky. Ct. App., June 13, 2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Supreme Court of Kentucky granted discretionary review of a decision of the Kentucky Court of Appeals, which held that district courts had no appellate jurisdiction and had to conduct a de novo trial as to a final order of the code enforcement board, but limiting the district court's review to making findings only from reviewing evidence introduced before the code enforcement board.


OVERVIEW: The issue was the scope of a district court's review of a decision of a local code enforcement board -- whether that review was de novo or of the record for an abuse of discretion in the form of an administrative appeal. Ky. Const. § 113 and Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann § 24A.010(3) specifically limited a district court's jurisdiction to original jurisdiction. Because a district court had no appellate jurisdiction, a review of the record for an abuse of discretion must proceed in circuit court, the first court with appellate jurisdiction. Thus, that part of Ky. Rev. Stat. § 65.8831(1), which limited an appeal in district court to a review of the record created before the code enforcement board was unconstitutional on its face for assigning appellate jurisdiction to a district court. The specific language, "The appeal shall be limited to a review of the record created before the code enforcement board." was therefore stricken. A district court could simply conduct a de novo trial per the statute, which would allow the court to supplement the record and hear arguments before ruling. Review of code enforcement board decisions were subject to de novo trials in district court.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed to the extent that it held that the district courts had no appellate jurisdiction and had to conduct a de novo trial as to the final order of the Code Enforcement Board; however, the judgment was reversed to the extent that it limited the district court's review to making findings only from reviewing evidence introduced before the Code Enforcement Board. The matter was remanded for further proceedings.

 

110 of 1068 DOCUMENTS


Ballard v. Commonwealth


2009-SC-000314-DG


SUPREME COURT OF KENTUCKY


320 S.W.3d 69; 2010 Ky. LEXIS 206



August 26, 2010, Rendered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication September 16, 2010.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
ON REVIEW FROM COURT OF APPEALS. CASE NO. 2008-CA-000791-MR. JEFFERSON CIRCUIT COURT NO. 04-CR-003183.
Commonwealth v. Ballard, 2009 Ky. App. Unpub. LEXIS 115 (Ky. Ct. App., Apr. 24, 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant pleaded guilty to various offenses and was sentenced to the felony pretrial diversion program. The Commonwealth filed a motion to remove him from pretrial diversion. the trial court dismissed the motion, stating that it lacked jurisdiction because the diversion period had ended. The Court of Appeals (Kentucky) reversed. Defendant sought discretionary review.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued, inter alia, that the court of appeals lacked jurisdiction to consider the Commonwealth's appeal from the order denying revocation of the diversion agreement. Defendant claimed that the court of appeals erred in characterizing the trial court's order as "final and appealable." The supreme court agreed. The trial court's order denied the motion to remove defendant from diversion because the trial court believed it lacked jurisdiction to do so. It did not purport to finally adjudicate the underlying charges. But, Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 22A.020(4) permitted the Commonwealth to appeal this interlocutory order. Defendant argued that § 22A.020(4) violated Ky. Const. § 2 because it conferred on the Commonwealth a right of appeal that the accused did not enjoy. The supreme court disagreed. The Commonwealth's right to appeal from an interlocutory order under Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 22A.020(4) was uniquely for the benefit of the Commonwealth. Nonetheless, the provision was reasonable because it furthered the Commonwealth's legitimate interest in the orderly administration of justice.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed in part and reversed in part.

 

111 of 1068 DOCUMENTS


Hayes v. Commonwealth


2009-SC-000087-MR


SUPREME COURT OF KENTUCKY


320 S.W.3d 93; 2010 Ky. LEXIS 185



August 26, 2010, Rendered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication September 16, 2010.
Related proceeding at Lucas v. Commonwealth, 2012 Ky. App. Unpub. LEXIS 422 (Ky. Ct. App., June 22, 2012)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
ON APPEAL FROM HARDIN CIRCUIT COURT. NO. 07-CR-00468. HONORABLE JANET P. COLEMAN, JUDGE.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from his Hardin Circuit Court (Kentucky) criminal convictions, arguing the trial court erred in: 1) adding ten additional jurors to the court's jury panel; 2) affecting the randomness of the jury selection process by holding the ten members back until needed; 3) failing to dismiss a juror for cause; and, 4) failing to grant his motion to suppress evidence seized on an unconstitutional search warrant.


OVERVIEW: The trial court did not substantially deviate from the prescribed jury selection procedures when it added the ten jurors from another division of the same circuit to its panel. The jury panel contemplated by Ky. R. Crim. P. 9.30 encompassed the entire jury pool for the trial courts of a particular jurisdiction, not just the members of one panel assigned for operational and management purposes to a particular court. When the citizens were summoned for jury service, they were for a county's court system as one judicial system and not just for one division or court. The randomness was not affected when the trial court held the ten members back. The trial court did not create a situation where anyone would be able to know the names of the members that would eventually compose the jury. The trial court did not err in failing to strike a juror for cause where the juror testified that neither a previous fight nor the murder of her relative would affect how she would evaluate the evidence or how she would judge this case. Finally, defendant failed to meet his burden of proving that the search warrant contained a false statement.


OUTCOME: Defendant's convictions were affirmed.
