Windsor v. Commonwealth


2008-SC-000383-MR


SUPREME COURT OF KENTUCKY


413 S.W.3d 568; 2010 Ky. LEXIS 312



August 26, 2010, Rendered 
March 29, 2011, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] 
CORRECTED: MARCH 29, 2011. MODIFIED: MARCH 24, 2011. CORRECTED: SEPTEMBER 20, 2010.


PRIOR HISTORY: ON APPEAL FROM JEFFERSON CIRCUIT COURT. HONORABLE MARTIN F. MCDONALD, JUDGE. NO. 04-CR-000001.
Windsor v. Commonwealth, 2010 Ky. LEXIS 210 (Ky., Aug. 26, 2010)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant challenged a decision from the Jefferson Circuit Court, Kentucky, which imposed two death sentences after an unconditional plea was entered to two counts of murder, one count of felony theft, and one count of violating a protective order.


OVERVIEW: Appellant admitted that he murdered his wife and son by stabbing them and beating them with a dumbbell. Appellant entered a plea of guilty and accepted a sentence of death. A sentencing hearing was conducted, and the death sentence was imposed. This appeal followed. In affirming, the supreme court determined that the trial court did not abuse its discretion by denying a motion under Ky. R. Crim. P. 8.06. Appellant had been found competent to stand trial just days earlier, and no new circumstances were presented that constituted reasonable grounds to question appellant's competency to plead guilty and request a death sentence. Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 532.025(3) did not require that a sentencing jury or judge find beyond a reasonable doubt that capital punishment was appropriate. Moreover, appellant was able to waive jury sentencing under Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 532.030(4), and there was no constitutional right to jury sentencing. A written transcript was not required for review because a video recording of the proceedings satisfied the requirements of Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 532.075. Re-sentencing was not warranted in this case.


OUTCOME: The decision was affirmed.

 

113 of 1068 DOCUMENTS


Cochran v. Commonwealth


2008-SC-000095-DG


SUPREME COURT OF KENTUCKY


315 S.W.3d 325; 2010 Ky. LEXIS 157



June 17, 2010, Rendered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication July 8, 2010.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
ON REVIEW FROM COURT OF APPEALS. CASE NO. 2006-CA-001561-MR. CASEY CIRCUIT COURT NO. 06-CR-00003.
Commonwealth v. Cochran, 2008 Ky. App. LEXIS 8 (Ky. Ct. App., Jan. 11, 2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After giving birth to a child who tested positive for cocaine, defendant was indicted for first-degree wanton endangerment, Ky. Rev. Stat. § 508.060. The trial court granted her motion to dismiss the indictment. The Kentucky Court of Appeals reversed the trial court's order dismissing the indictment. The Kentucky Supreme Court granted discretionary review.


OVERVIEW: Citing Commonwealth v. Welch and the Maternal Health Act of 1992, 1992 Ky. Acts 442 (H.B. 192) (Act), the court concluded that the criminal abuse statutes did not apply to a woman's conduct during pregnancy. To find otherwise would have created a situation in which a pregnant woman's conduct would have been subject to an indefinite number of new crimes covering the full range of a behavior - rendering the statutes void for vagueness. The legislature did not intend criminal sanctions for prenatal use of drugs and alcohol. The preamble of the Act clearly stated that taking punitive actions taken against pregnant alcohol or substance abusers would have created additional problems, including discouraging those individuals from seeking the essential prenatal care and substance abuse treatment necessary to deliver a healthy newborn. The court was not at liberty to ignore the legislative intent clearly expressed in the Act. The conduct alleged in the indictment did not constitute a crime; hence, the indictment was invalid on its face and properly dismissed.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the opinion of the Court of Appeals and reinstated the dismissal of the indictment by the trial court.

Commonwealth v. Chauvin


2008-SC-000509-MR


SUPREME COURT OF KENTUCKY


316 S.W.3d 279; 2010 Ky. LEXIS 146



June 17, 2010, Rendered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication July 8, 2010.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
ON APPEAL FROM COURT OF APPEALS. CASE NO. 2008-CA-000027-OA. JEFFERSON CIRCUIT COURT NO. 06-CI-009152.
Commonwealth v. Chauvin, 2008 Ky. App. LEXIS 190 (Ky. Ct. App., June 13, 2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant Commonwealth appealed from an order of the Kentucky Court of Appeals denying in part and granting in part the Commonwealth's petition for a writ of prohibition.


OVERVIEW: The defendant in a civil suit sought discovery of a Kentucky All-Schedule Prescription Electronic Reporting (KASPER) record on the plaintiff in the suit. These records were held by the Cabinet for Health and Family Services, which compiled them pursuant to Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 218A.202. The Commonwealth sought a writ of prohibition to bar enforcement of the order to disclose the materials. The circuit court ordered that the plaintiff's KASPER record be conditionally released to the defendant's attorney. The appellate court ordered the circuit court to first conduct an in camera review to determine what parts of the records, if any, were relevant discovery material before allowing any disclosure to the parties. The instant court concluded that the state legislature had the power to create privileges, such as to KASPER records, both from the instant court's own rules and from the legislature's inherent power to enact substantive law. Thus, the appellate court erred in finding that the KASPER statute violated the separation of powers doctrine. The KASPER statute created a privilege as to KASPER records, which the circuit court's discovery order would have violated.


OUTCOME: The order of the appellate court was reversed, and the case was remanded with instructions to issue the writ requested to bar enforcement of the circuit court's discovery order.

Spencer v. Estate of Spencer


2008-SC-000191-DG AND 2008-SC-000196-DG


SUPREME COURT OF KENTUCKY


313 S.W.3d 534; 2010 Ky. LEXIS 154



June 17, 2010, Rendered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication July 8, 2010.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
ON REVIEW FROM COURT OF APPEALS. CASE NO. 2007-CA-000277-MR. McCRACKEN CIRCUIT COURT NO. 06-CI-00396.
Estate of Spencer v. Spencer, 2008 Ky. App. LEXIS 37 (Ky. Ct. App., Feb. 15, 2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellee estate filed a declaratory judgment action against appellant widow to determine the ownership of a brokerage account. A trial court ruled that the account was a joint account with the right of survivorship and that on the decedent's death it passed entirely to the widow. The Kentucky Court of Appeals rejected that analysis and held that the decedent and the widow held the brokerage account as tenants in common. Both parties appealed.


OVERVIEW: The estate maintained that an ante-nuptial agreement between the decedent and the widow precluded her from having any interest in the brokerage account. The widow argued that the trial court correctly ruled that the entire brokerage account passed to her under the right of survivorship. The ante-nuptial agreement did provide that neither would claim any interest in the pre-marital property of the other. However, the court found that the agreement did not foreclose gifts from the decedent to the widow. Further, while the nature of the decedent's gift to the widow was to be determined on remand, the agreement's requirement that the giver's intention be clearly memorialized was satisfied by the decedent's letter of authorization creating the joint account, notwithstanding the fact that the letter was not attached to the agreement. The statutory presumption on which the widow relied, that "joint accounts" under Ky. Rev. Stat. § 391.315 included the right of survivorship, did not apply to brokerage accounts. Because neither party was entitled to summary judgment, the dispute over the ownership of the account should have been remanded to the trial court for additional proceedings.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the court of appeals' opinion that neither party was entitled to summary judgment on the grounds they alleged but reversed the portion of the opinion granting summary judgment on different grounds as to the ownership of the brokerage account. The court remanded the matter to the trial court for additional proceedings consistent with the court's opinion.

Brown v. Commonwealth


2006-SC-000654-MR


SUPREME COURT OF KENTUCKY


313 S.W.3d 577; 2010 Ky. LEXIS 148



June 17, 2010, Rendered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication July 8, 2010.

Post-conviction relief denied at Brown v. Commonwealth, 2015 Ky. App. Unpub. LEXIS 531 (Ky. Ct. App., July 24, 2015)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
APPEAL FROM WARREN CIRCUIT COURT. HONORABLE JAMES G. WEDDLE, SPECIAL JUDGE. NO. 06-CR-00248.
Brown v. Commonwealth, 2005 Ky. Unpub. LEXIS 4 (Ky., Aug. 25, 2005)



DISPOSITION:  The death sentence was vacated, and the case was remanded with instructions to resentence defendant for murder. Otherwise, the judgment of conviction and sentence was affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Warren Circuit Court (Kentucky) that convicted him of first-degree burglary, first-degree robbery, and murder and that sentenced him to consecutive 20-year prison terms for the former offenses and death for the murder.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of the three offenses in 2003; in that trial, the jury found aggravating circumstances but did not recommend the death penalty. Instead, the jury recommended a life sentence. Defendant challenged his conviction, and the court reversed. On retrial, defendant was again convicted and this time was sentenced to death. On review, the court held that, under controlling Double Jeopardy precedent, because defendant's first trial concluded with a verdict fixing punishment at a life sentence, the Commonwealth was precluded from seeking the death penalty a second time. However, the trial court did not err under Ky. Const. § 11 in refusing to strike a prospective juror who was a law enforcement officer and whose father and brother were law enforcement officers because her personal and familial connections with law enforcement did not render her implicitly biased in favor of the Commonwealth. Because the prospective juror had no personal acquaintance with the attorneys or police officers involved in the case and betrayed no tendency to lend heightened credence to police testimony, the trial court did not abuse its discretion by refusing to disqualify the juror.


OUTCOME: The death sentence was vacated, and the case was remanded with instructions to resentence defendant for murder. Otherwise, the judgment of conviction and sentence was affirmed.

Members Choice Credit Union v. Home Fed. S&L Ass'n


2008-SC-000877-DG


SUPREME COURT OF KENTUCKY


323 S.W.3d 658; 2010 Ky. LEXIS 125



May 20, 2010, Rendered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication November 18, 2010.
Rehearing denied by Members Choice Credit Union v. Home Fed. S&L Ass'n, 2010 Ky. LEXIS 356 (Ky., Nov. 18, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
ON REVIEW FROM COURT OF APPEALS. CASE NOS. 2007-CA-002353-MR AND 2007-CA-002384-MR. FRANKLIN CIRCUIT COURT NO. 06-CI-00737.
Members Choice Credit Union v. Home Fed. S&L Ass'n, 2009 Ky. LEXIS 724 (Ky., Feb. 11, 2009)
Commonwealth ex rel. Office of Fin. Insts. v. Home Fed. Savs. & Loan Ass'n, 2008 Ky. App. LEXIS 343 (Ky. Ct. App., Oct. 31, 2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Franklin Circuit Court, Kentucky, granted summary judgment in favor of appellee savings and loan association on its petition for a declaratory judgment against the Department of Financial Institutions (DFI). Appellant credit unions alleged that they had previously been granted permission by DFI to amend their bylaws and to allow geographic fields of membership. The Kentucky Court of Appeals affirmed. The credit unions appealed.


OVERVIEW: The supreme court noted that the sole question was whether the current version of the credit union membership statute, Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 286.6-107, allowed geographic fields of membership for state-chartered credit unions. The supreme court found that the association's argument that the terms "association" and "interest" should be read as excluding geographic and other traditionally accepted fields of membership because of the departure from the model act was not convincing. The credit unions could not realistically argue that they fell under the association category. The term was not so expansive as to include mere geographical proximity. It did not include the traditional community field of membership. The credit unions did fit under the interest category. Persons who lived in the same neighborhood or rural farming district did have a concrete shared interest that demonstrated a common bond. Thus, a geographic or community field of membership was allowed under the current language of § 286.6-107.


OUTCOME: The court of appeals was reversed and the matter was remanded for entry of judgment.

Star v. Commonwealth


2008-SC-000203-MR


SUPREME COURT OF KENTUCKY


313 S.W.3d 30; 2010 Ky. LEXIS 115



May 20, 2010, Rendered

PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
ON APPEAL FROM JOHNSON CIRCUIT COURT. HONORABLE JOHN DAVID PRESTON, JUDGE. NO. 07-CR-00103.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Johnson Circuit Court (Kentucky) finding him guilty but mentally ill of two murders, one kidnapping, and one assault.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, the court held that defendant was not entitled to a directed verdict because it was not clearly unreasonable for the jury to find against defendant on the issue of insanity under Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 504.020 because, although defendant had a history of mental illness and had been previously diagnosed with paranoid schizophrenia, the evidence showed that: (1) defendant admitting to shooting the three victims under the false impression that two of them had attempted to poison him; (2) defendant testified that he walked away from the shooting because he was looking for an escape route; (3) defendant told onlookers to tell the police that they could get him in the morning because he wanted to get some rest; (4) one victim testified that although defendant intended to shoot her a second time, she asked him not to and he complied; (5) a psychiatrist who interviewed defendant a few days after the shootings stated that he knew it was wrong and illegal to kill someone; and (6) the court-appointed psychiatrist testified that defendant showed an ability to control his actions because he did not attempt to murder other people he also believed were poisoning him.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Fox v. Grayson


2009-SC-000066-TG


SUPREME COURT OF KENTUCKY


317 S.W.3d 1; 2010 Ky. LEXIS 88



April 22, 2010, Rendered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication August 26, 2010.
Rehearing denied by Fox v. Grayson, 2010 Ky. LEXIS 224 (Ky., Aug. 26, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
ON APPEAL FROM FRANKLIN CIRCUIT COURT. HONORABLE THOMAS D. WINGATE, JUDGE. NO. 08-CI-01426.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant appointed council member sought review of a decision of the Franklin Circuit Court (Kentucky), which dismissed her declaratory action against appellees, the secretary of state and governor of Kentucky, and a nominee. Appellant argued that the appointment of the nominee to the education council was ineffective because appellant had been confirmed to the position by the state senate.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was appointed to the education council by one governor and when another governor was elected during appellant's term, he appointed the nominee to the same position while the general assembly was not in session. Appellant claimed that Ky. Const. § 93 vested the senate with the sole power to confirm appointees. The court reversed and held that Ky. Const. § 93 was ambiguous and that the inclusion of language permitting the senate to confirm nominees led to a strong presumption that the house was intentionally excluded from the confirmation process. The court found that the application of the expressio unius interpretive maxim led to a conclusion that the senate alone had the constitutional confirmation power under § 93. Also, the most logical conclusion was that the term "may" in § 93 signified only that the general assembly had the permissive discretion to choose which gubernatorial appointees must be subjected to a confirmation. However, while the trial court erred in dismissing appellant's complaint, the ultimate merits of were an entirely separate matter, which the parties had not yet had a full opportunity to either prove or defend.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the circuit court's order dismissing appellant's complaint and remanded the matter to the trial court.

Univ. of the Cumberlands v. Pennybacker


2008-SC-000253-TG, (2008-CA-000643), 2008-SC-000285-TG, (2008-CA-000682)


SUPREME COURT OF KENTUCKY


308 S.W.3d 668; 2010 Ky. LEXIS 97



April 22, 2010, Rendered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for publication May 13, 2010.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
ON APPEAL FROM FRANKLIN CIRCUIT COURT. HONORABLE ROGER L. CRITTENDEN. NO. 06-CI-00554.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants, a university and members of the Kentucky General Assembly, challenged a judgment of the Franklin Circuit Court (Kentucky), in an action filed by plaintiffs, an alliance, a teachers' association, and taxpayers, holding that a $ 10 million appropriation for the construction of pharmacy school building on the university's campus and a $ 1 million appropriation for a pharmacy scholarship program violated the state constitution.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, the court held that the pharmacy school appropriation for the university violated Ky. Const. § 189 because it was an allocation of public funds for educational purposes to a church, sectarian, or denominational school, and the university was a Baptist university that had been supported by members and churches of the Baptist faith. The court further held that § 189 did not offend the First Amendment by prohibiting appropriations of public tax monies to religious schools because there is no speech forum at issue where defendants allege discrimination in the expenditure of public funds for education. The court held that Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 164.7901, which provided scholarship money to individual students who attended the university's pharmacy school, was a special legislation in contravention of Ky. Const. § 59 because in restricting scholarships to those attending the university's pharmacy school, the Kentucky General Assembly failed to treat equally all members of the pharmacy student class, precisely the type of special privilege and favoritism that § 59 condemned.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Jones v. Commonwealth


2007-SC-000922-DG


SUPREME COURT OF KENTUCKY


319 S.W.3d 295; 2010 Ky. LEXIS 99



April 22, 2010, Rendered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Modified and rehearing denied by Jones v. Commonwealth, 2010 Ky. LEXIS 250 (Ky., Sept. 23, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
ON REVIEW FROM COURT OF APPEALS. CASE NOS. 2006-CA-000897-MR AND 2006-CA-001792-MR. HICKMAN CIRCUIT COURT NO. 00-CR-00001 AND CALLOWAY CIRCUIT COURT NO. 01-CR-00128.
Jones v. Commonwealth, 2007 Ky. App. Unpub. LEXIS 738 (Ky. Ct. App., Nov. 9, 2007)
Henley v. Commonwealth, 2007 Ky. App. LEXIS 427 (Ky. Ct. App., Nov. 9, 2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Before the court were two consolidated cases from the Kentucky Court of Appeals with the same issue: whether Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 532.043(5) violated the separation of powers doctrine of Ky. Const. §§ 27 and 28 by giving the judicial branch, rather than the executive branch, the power to revoke conditional discharge imposed after a period of incarceration. In both cases, the revocation of appellants' conditional discharge had been affirmed.


OVERVIEW: Both appellants served out their initial sentences. Upon release, appellants were placed on three years conditional discharge and referred to the Division of Probation and Parole for supervision. Both appellants violated the conditions of conditional discharge and were brought back to their respective sentencing courts for a revocation hearing. Appellants each argued to their trial court that the revocation procedure established § 532.043(5) violated the separation of powers doctrine. The court agreed with appellants. Section 532.042(5) violated §§ 27 and 28 of the Kentucky Constitution by impermissibly conferring an executive power to revoke a post-incarceration or post-parole conditional release upon the judiciary. Section 28 precluded one branch from exercising any power belonging to the other branches. Only on appeal of an administrative action should the judicial branch become involved in the executive branch's legitimate exercise of its power to execute sentences. The court noted that its ruling was limited to the revocation procedure in subsection (5). Because subsection (5) was severable from the remainder of the statute, the statute's other provisions remained in force.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the revocations in both cases and remanded the cases to their respective circuit courts for proceedings consistent with the court's opinion.

 

122 of 1068 DOCUMENTS



Blue Movies, Inc. v. Louisville/Jefferson County Metro Gov't


2007-SC-000812-DG


SUPREME COURT OF KENTUCKY


317 S.W.3d 23; 2010 Ky. LEXIS 101



April 22, 2010, Rendered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication August 26, 2010.
Rehearing denied by Blue Movies, Inc. v. Louisville/Jefferson County Metro Gov't, 2010 Ky. LEXIS 228 (Ky., Aug. 26, 2010)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Blue Movies, Inc. v. Metro. Gov't, 2011 U.S. LEXIS 1991 (U.S., Mar. 7, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
ON REVIEW FROM COURT OF APPEALS. CASE NO. 2005-CA-000085-MR. CASE NO. 2005-CA-000090-MR. CASE NO. 2005-CA-000091-MR. CASE NO. 2005-CA-000092-MR. CASE NO. 2005-CA-000100-MR. CASE NO. 2005-CA-000113-MR. CASE NO. 2005-CA-000176-MR. JEFFERSON CIRCUIT COURT NO. 04-CI-01967.
Cam I, Inc. v. Louisville/Jefferson County Metro Gov't, 2007 Ky. App. LEXIS 370 (Ky. Ct. App., Oct. 5, 2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, adult entertainment business operators (operators), challenged a judgment of the Court of Appeals of Kentucky holding that appellee county's 2004 amendments to its code of ordinances, which placed numerous restrictions on adult entertainment businesses, were constitutional.


OVERVIEW: On appeal, the court held that the county ordinance's "no touch" provision was unconstitutionally overbroad under the First Amendment because prohibiting all touching between adult establishment customers and entertainers, including benign, nonsexual touching, was substantially broader than necessary to achieve the county's interest in combating prostitution and sexually transmitted diseases. An ordinance could be easily be more narrowly tailored to prohibit sexual touching, as in a ban on touching during a performance or while in a state of nudity. However, the court held that the provision prohibiting customers from directly tipping the entertainers was not unconstitutional because it did not deprive the entertainers of the ability to earn tips, as they could still be earned via a tip jar placed at the edge of the six-foot buffer zone. The provision prohibiting the sale of alcohol did not violate the 21st Amendment because under the Amendment, the State had broad power to regulate the times, places, and circumstances under which liquor could be sold.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed insofar as it held that the blanket "no touch" provision was constitutional. The judgment was otherwise affirmed and the case was remanded for further proceedings.


 

123 of 1068 DOCUMENTS


St. Clair v. Commonwealth


2005-SC-000828-MR


SUPREME COURT OF KENTUCKY


319 S.W.3d 300; 2010 Ky. LEXIS 90



April 22, 2010, Rendered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Modified and rehearing denied by St. Clair v. Commonwealth, 2010 Ky. LEXIS 249 (Ky., Sept. 23, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
ON APPEAL FROM BULLITT CIRCUIT COURT. HONORABLE THOMAS WALLER, JUDGE. NO. 92-CR-00010-002.
St. Clair v. Commonwealth, 140 S.W.3d 510, 2004 Ky. LEXIS 44 (Ky., 2004)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: A jury for the Bullitt Circuit Court (Kentucky) found defendant guilty of capital murder. The trial court sentenced defendant to death in accordance with the jury's recommendation. On appeal, the supreme court affirmed his capital murder conviction but reversed his death sentence and remanded the case to the trial court for a new capital sentencing trial. Following the new sentencing trial, defendant was again sentenced to death. He appealed.


OVERVIEW: Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 532.025(2)(a)(1) required that defendant actually have the prior record of conviction for a capital offense at the time the instant offense of murder (or kidnapping) was committed. The trial court's instruction could have erroneously allowed a jury to find that aggravating factor even if defendant did not have a prior record of conviction of a capital offense at the time the instant offense was committed but, simply, had accrued such a prior record of conviction by the time of trial. Because the jury was presented with evidence of many convictions for capital offenses, some of which pre-dated the commission of the current murder and some of which did not, the instruction, as given, also deprived defendant of his right to a unanimous verdict. To avoid any possibility that the jury relied upon a non-qualifying conviction in finding the aggravator, in defendant's case and in similar future cases, the jury instruction had to require that the jury specifically identify the conviction that it used to find the aggravator. By specifically identifying qualifying convictions, the trial court could ensure that the jury did not rely on convictions that could not qualify.


OUTCOME: The supreme court reversed and vacated defendant's the death sentence and remanded the case for resentencing proceedings in conformity with the supreme court's opinion.

 

124 of 1068 DOCUMENTS



Lassister v. Am. Express Travel Related Servs. Co.


2008-SC-000904-DG


SUPREME COURT OF KENTUCKY


308 S.W.3d 714; 2010 Ky. LEXIS 93



April 22, 2010, Rendered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for publication May 13, 2010.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
ON REVIEW FROM COURT OF APPEALS. CASE NOS. 2007-CA-000908-MR AND 2007-CA-000973-MR. FRANKLIN CIRCUIT COURT NO. 06-CI-01151.
Lassiter v. Am. Express Travel Related Servs. Co., 2008 Ky. App. Unpub. LEXIS 77 (Ky. Ct. App., Oct. 3, 2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, the Kentucky State Budget Director, sought review of a decision of the Kentucky Court of Appeals, which had dismissed her appeal because she failed to name an indispensible party to the appeal. Appellee, a financial institution, had set forth the action by alleging that a provision in an executive branch budget bill that shortened the escheat period for unredeemed traveler's checks for a two-year budget period was unconstitutional.


OVERVIEW: The Director contended that the lower court erred by dismissing her appeal for failure to name the State Treasurer as a party to the appeal. She claimed that her notice of appeal did effectively name the Treasurer by having named the Department of Treasury in the caption. The court agreed and held that because the Department of Treasury did not itself appeal, as a non-appealing party, it was properly listed in the appellee's position in the caption. It was determined that naming a party in the caption of the notice was, standing alone, sufficient to satisfy the notice requirement even though the party was not named in the body of the notice. There was no other reasonable purpose for listing the Department of Treasury in the caption except to name it as a party to the appeal. As such, the listing gave fair notice to the financial institution that the Director was naming the Department of Treasury as a party to the appeal. Accordingly, the lower court erred by dismissing the appeal for failure to name the Treasurer as a party to the appeal.


OUTCOME: The decision was reversed and the matter was remanded for a decision on the appeal's merits.

Petitioner F v. Brown


2008-SC-000213-DG


SUPREME COURT OF KENTUCKY


306 S.W.3d 80; 2010 Ky. LEXIS 70



March 18, 2010, Rendered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication April 8, 2010.
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Petitioner G v. Brown, 562 U.S. 985, 131 S. Ct. 422, 178 L. Ed. 2d 329, 2010 U.S. LEXIS 8178 (2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
ON REVIEW FROM COURT OF APPEALS. CASE NO. 2006-CA-002450-MR. FRANKLIN CIRCUIT COURT NO. 06-CI-00214.
Petitioner F v. Brown, 2008 Ky. App. Unpub. LEXIS 532 (Ky. Ct. App., Feb. 22, 2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, juveniles adjudicated to be public offenders for various sex offenses, challenged a judgment of the Kentucky Court of Appeals that affirmed a circuit court order and held that Kentucky's DNA sampling statutes, Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. §§17.170 through 17.175 (2002), required them to submit DNA for inclusion in a state and national database, arguing tha the requirement violated their rights under the Fourth Amendment, U.S. Const. amend. IV.


OVERVIEW: Kentucky's DNA sampling statutes require convicted felons to submit DNA samples for inclusion in a database to help law enforcement agencies identify perpetrators of violent crimes. After the appellate court ruled that juveniles adjudicated public offenders did not fall within the purview of those statutes because an adjudicated juvenile had not been convicted of a felony, the legislature amended the statutes, expanding the class of persons subject to DNA sampling to include certain juveniles adjudicated public offenders. After the juveniles in the instant case were adjudicated delinquent for committing various sex crimes, they were ordered to submit a DNA sample. They petitioned for a writ of prohibition, but the circuit court found in favor of appellee, the Commissioner of the Kentucky Department of Juvenile Justice (DJJ), and the appellate court affirmed. On review, the court held that the Fourth Amendment privacy interests of juveniles adjudicated public offenders, while greater than those of convicted adults, did not outweigh the State's and DJJ's legitimate interests in establishing and maintaining a DNA database to aid in solving crimes.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the appellate court was affirmed.

Foley v. Commonwealth

Foley v. Commonwealth, 942 S.W.2d 876, 43:12 Ky. L. Summary 11, 1996 Ky. LEXIS 127 (Ky., 1997)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant inmate challenged a decision from the Franklin Circuit Court, Kentucky, which denied his petition for a declaratory judgment under Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 418.040.


OVERVIEW: The inmate sought to have Kentucky's self-defense statutes, as they existed at the time of his 1991 trial, declared unconstitutional under Ky. Const. § 1. The trial court determined that there was no actual case or controversy as stated in the declaratory judgment petition. The trial court dismissed the petition for failure to state a claim upon which relief could have been granted. In affirming, the supreme court determined that the petition failed to plead an existing actual controversy. The constitutionality of the self-defense statutes had no foreseeable application to the inmate; he was incarcerated in a maximum security prison subject to six death sentences. The adjudication of this issue would have amounted to no more than an advisory opinion. It was noted that the declaratory judgment procedure was excluded from the post-conviction process. Piecemeal litigation though successive declaratory judgment actions was precluded.


OUTCOME: The decision was affirmed.

Beshear v. Haydon Bridge Co.


2007-SC-000058-TG


SUPREME COURT OF KENTUCKY


304 S.W.3d 682; 2010 Ky. LEXIS 8



January 21, 2010, Rendered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication February 11, 2010.
Subsequent appeal at Beshear v. Haydon Bridge Co., 2013 Ky. LEXIS 582 (Ky., Nov. 21, 2013)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
ON APPEAL FROM FRANKLIN CIRCUIT COURT. HONORABLE WILLIAM L. GRAHAM, JUDGE. NO. 03-CI-01547.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellees, employers subject to the Kentucky Workers' Compensation Act, sought a declaration of rights and injunctive relief against appellants, the Governor of Kentucky and the State Budget Director, contending that budgetary actions violated Ky. Const. § 15, 51, and 180. The trial court (Kentucky) granted the employers' motion for partial summary judgment. The parties' cross-appeals were transferred from the Court of Appeals (Kentucky).


OVERVIEW: The employers complained about transfers of millions of dollars from the Kentucky Workers' Compensation Funding Commission (KWCFC) and the Workers' Compensation Benefit Reserve Fund (BRF) to the general fund and to the mining budget. The trial court upheld the questioned budgetary acts under Ky. Const. § 180 but invalidated them under Ky. Const. § 51. On appeal, the court held that the annual transfer of $ 19 million to the general fund did not violate Ky. Const. § 180, but it held that the other transfers were invalid because the funds were commingled and could not be differentiated. Under Armstrong, the transfer of funds actually held in agency accounts in which public funds and private contributions were commingled and could not be differentiated, could not be considered a valid suspension of the operation of a statute under Ky. Const. § 15 and Ky. Const. § 51. Nevertheless, the transfer or diversion of funds which had not yet been received by the KWCFC and BRF were not commingled agency funds. Finally, the continuance of the employers' assessment rate of 11.5 percent was not a tax subject to the publication provision of Ky. Const. § 51.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the determination that the budget acts were valid under Ky. Const. § 180 and its decision supported by Armstrong. It reversed the decision under Ky. Const. § 51 that the legislature could not properly (1) suspend the $ 19 million annual appropriation to the KWCFC and the BRF to the extent the funds had yet to be transferred to them, or (2) mandate the continuance of the employers' assessment rate. It then remanded the matter.

Hunt v. Commonwealth


2006-SC-000634-MR


SUPREME COURT OF KENTUCKY


304 S.W.3d 15; 2009 Ky. LEXIS 346



November 25, 2009, Rendered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [**1] 
Released for Publication March 18, 2010.
As Modified March 18, 2010.
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Hunt v. Kentucky, 2010 U.S. LEXIS 7060 (U.S., Oct. 4, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY: ON APPEAL FROM FLOYD CIRCUIT COURT. HONORABLE JOHN DAVID CAUDILL, JUDGE. NO. 05-CR-00090.
Hunt v. Commonwealth, 2009 Ky. LEXIS 292 (Ky., Nov. 25, 2009)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Floyd Circuit Court (Kentucky) that convicted him of murder, first-degree burglary, and first-degree wanton endangerment, arguing that the trial court erred in failing to instruct the jury on criminal trespass as a lesser-included offense of burglary and erred in failing to granted a judgment of directed verdict in his favor on the endangerment charge.


OVERVIEW: Defendant his wife were estranged, and a source of contention between them was that his wife frequently cared for her premature granddaughter, who suffered from severe health problems. Defendant went to home where his wife was living and fired numerous shots, killing her. On review, the court held that the trial court did not err in refusing to instruct the jury on criminal trespass under Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 511.060 as a lesser-included offense of burglary under Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 511.060 because the evidence was overwhelming that defendant intended to commit a crime; if defendant did not intent to commit murder, he at least intended to harass, menace, or wantonly endanger the victim. Further, the trial court did not err in refusing to grant a directed verdict on the endangerment charge under Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 508.060 because the murder occurred in close proximity to the bassinet in which the infant lay and bullets were found near the bassinet. Because bullets could ricochet and because evidence established that defendant was intoxicated when he fired the gun, the evidence supported the denial of his motion for a directed verdict.


OUTCOME: The judgment of conviction and the imposition of the death penalty were affirmed.

Commonwealth ex rel. Conway v. Thompson


2009-SC-000107-TG, 2009-SC-000252-TG


SUPREME COURT OF KENTUCKY


300 S.W.3d 152; 2009 Ky. LEXIS 290



November 25, 2009, Rendered 
November 25, 2009, Entered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication January 4, 2010.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
ON APPEAL FROM FRANKLIN CIRCUIT COURT. HONORABLE PHILLIP J. SHEPHERD, JUDGE. NO. 08-CI-01651. ON APPEAL FROM PULASKI CIRCUIT COURT. HONORABLE DAVID A. TAPP, JUDGE. NO. 08-CI-01201.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Franklin Circuit Court, Kentucky, refused appellant Kentucky Attorney General's request for a temporary injunction, which would have temporarily enjoined appellee Kentucky Department of Corrections (DOC) from implementing House Bill (HB) 406 in an allegedly retroactive manner. The Pulaski Circuit court granted a permanent injunction prohibiting DOC from releasing any prisoner or parolee. The case was transferred to the supreme court.


OVERVIEW: The supreme court had to determine whether the trial court erred when it concluded that the DOC improperly applied HB 406, 2008 Ky. Acts 127, retroactively. The supreme court noted that Pulaski Circuit Court had jurisdiction over both the subject matter and the parties involved in the action. It had powers co-extensive with the Franklin Circuit Court to adjudicate the matter. Since the General Assembly appropriated even less money than the DOC projected to save through the retroactive application of the street time credit, there could be no legitimate contention that more spending was intended. The Pulaski Circuit Court erred when it determined that the General Assembly did not intend HB 406 to have retroactive effect. A writ of prohibition should issue against the Pulaski Circuit Court. The Attorney General had a sufficient personal right in these types of cases by virtue of the office and the duties commensurate with that high office. The information provided to juries under Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 532.055 was unaffected by HB 406. There was no separation of powers violation, Ky. Const. §§ 27 and 28. HB 406 did not violate Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 197.045.


OUTCOME: The writ against the Pulaski Circuit Court was granted; the Franklin Circuit Court's denial of a temporary injunction was affirmed.

Bowling v. Ky. Dep't of Corr.


2007-SC-000021-MR


SUPREME COURT OF KENTUCKY


301 S.W.3d 478; 2009 Ky. LEXIS 291



November 25, 2009, Rendered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication January 4, 2010.
Post-conviction relief denied at Bowling v. Commonwealth, 2010 Ky. LEXIS 242 (Ky., Sept. 23, 2010)
Writ denied by, Motion denied by, As moot, Sub nomine at Commonwealth v. Shepherd, 2011 Ky. LEXIS 31 (Ky., Mar. 24, 2011)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
ON APPEAL FROM FRANKLIN CIRCUIT COURT. HONORABLE SAMUEL G. MCNAMARA, JUDGE. CASE NO. 06-CI-00574.
Baze v. Commonwealth, 276 S.W.3d 761, 2008 Ky. LEXIS 297 (Ky., 2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Franklin Circuit Court, Kentucky, convicted defendants of murder and sentenced them to death in 1991 and 1994. The circuit court rejected defendants' challenge to Kentucky's lethal injection protocol on several grounds including the prohibition on cruel and unusual punishment, U.S. Const. amend. VIII and Ky. Const. § 17. Defendants appealed.


OVERVIEW: Two defendants argued that the lethal injection protocol was unenforceable because it was not properly adopted as an administrative regulation in accordance with Kentucky's Administrative Procedure Act (APA). The supreme court found that they were required to join all claims regarding implementation of their sentences of execution in their original declaratory judgment action, and they were precluded from reserving arguments for subsequent actions. Res judicata barred consideration of the second declaratory judgment action filed by two defendants. The third defendant had not previously filed an action challenging the implementation of his death sentence and consequently his APA claim was cognizable. The APA mandated that the lethal injection protocol be promulgated as a regulation to the extent if affected private rights. It had to be properly adopted pursuant to Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. ch. 13A before appellee Kentucky Department of Corrections proceeded with further executions. When a matter had to be prescribed by administrative regulation pursuant to Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 13A.100, the APA had to be complied with in all respects.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed as to the dismissal of two defendants' claims because of res judicata; the judgment was reversed as to the third defendant as the Department of Corrections was required to promulgate a regulation as to all portions of the lethal injection protocol.

Cecil v. Commonwealth


2008-SC-000159-MR AND 2008-SC-000369-MR


SUPREME COURT OF KENTUCKY


297 S.W.3d 12; 2009 Ky. LEXIS 245



October 29, 2009, Rendered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication November 19, 2009.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
ON APPEAL FROM JEFFERSON CIRCUIT COURT. HONORABLE GEOFFREY P. MORRIS, JUDGE. NO. 06-CR-002483.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Jefferson Circuit Court (Kentucky) that convicted him of two counts of rape in the first degree in violation of Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 510.040 and one count of intimidating a participant in the legal process under Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 524.040(1)(f), arguing that the trial court erred in failing to instruct the jury on lesser-included offenses and in imposing consecutive sentences.


OVERVIEW: On review, the court held that the trial court did not err in refusing to instruct the jury on first-degree sexual abuse under Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 510.110(1)(b)(2) as a lesser-included offense of rape because the former was not a lesser-included offense where the young victim's testimony was unequivocal that defendant inserted his penis into her vagina and defendant did not controvert her testimony. Although the victim also testified regarding an instance during which defendant removed her clothing and touched her vagina but did not rape her, this testimony did not warrant a lesser-included offense instruction because the testimony did not contradict the testimony of sexual penetration but was merely testimony regarding a separate, uncharged offense. However, error occurred in sentencing defendant because the retroactive application of Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 532.110(1)(d) was prohibited by the ex post facto clauses of the state and federal constitutions, Ky. Const. § 19 and U.S. Const. art. I, § 9. By removing the possibility of concurrent sentences, § 532.110(1)(d) created a new standard of punishment that was more onerous than that under the former statute.


OUTCOME: Defendant's convictions were affirmed, but the case was remanded for a new sentencing hearing.

Emberton v. GMRI, Inc.


2007-SC-000443-DG & 2008-SC-000109-DG


SUPREME COURT OF KENTUCKY


299 S.W.3d 565; 2009 Ky. LEXIS 250



October 29, 2009, Rendered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication January 21, 2010.
Rehearing denied by Emberton v. GMRI, Inc., 2010 Ky. LEXIS 494 (Ky., Jan. 21, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
ON REVIEW FROM COURT OF APPEALS. CASE NOS. 2005-CA-002355-MR & 2005-CA-002517-MR. WARREN CIRCUIT COURT NO. 04-CI-01213.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant restaurant customer challenged a decision of the Kentucky Court of Appeals that reversed a circuit court judgment denying appellee restaurant's motion to dismiss on the basis that the customer's suit was time barred by the statute of limitations.


OVERVIEW: The customer filed suit against the restaurant after contracting hepatitis A. The restaurant moved to dismiss under the statute of limitations because the customer's suit was filed three years after he fell ill. The circuit court denied the motion, ruling that the statute of limitations was tolled because the restaurant actively concealed the customer's cause of action. The court of appeals reversed, ruling that the suit was barred under Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 413.140(1)(a) because it was not filed within one year of the alleged negligence. On review, the court held that the statute of limitations was tolled by operation of Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 413.190(2) due to the restaurant's active concealment of an employee's hygiene, work habits, and hepatitis A infection. The record demonstrated that the restaurant manager suppressed the fact of the employee's hepatitis A infection in responding to a health department investigation; as such, the statute of limitations was tolled for the entire period of concealment. Because the customer filed suit within three months of learning that his illness resulted from unsanitary conditions at the restaurant, his suit was timely filed.


OUTCOME: The decision of the court of appeals was reversed, and the judgment of the circuit court was reinstated.

 

133 of 1068 DOCUMENTS


Commonwealth v. Baker


2007-SC-000347-CL


SUPREME COURT OF KENTUCKY


295 S.W.3d 437; 2009 Ky. LEXIS 233



October 1, 2009, Rendered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication November 2, 2009.
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Kentucky v. Baker, 2010 U.S. LEXIS 2267 (U.S., Mar. 8, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
FROM KENTON DISTRICT COURT. HONORABLE MARTIN J. SHEEHAN, JUDGE. NO. 07-M-00604.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Kenton District Court, Kentucky, concluded that Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 17.545, the sex offender registration statute, as applied to defendant, violated the ex post facto clauses of the United States and Kentucky Constitutions. The Commonwealth moved the Kentucky Supreme Court for certification of law to determine whether the statute was an ex post fact punishment.


OVERVIEW: The supreme court noted that to violate the ex post facto clause, U.S. Const. art. I, § 10 and Ky. Const. § 19(1), Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 17.545 had to be punitive. The supreme court concluded that the General Assembly intended § 17.545 to be a civil, nonpunitive, regulatory scheme. The residency restrictions in the statute had been regarded in Kentucky history and traditions as punishment. By imposing restraints based solely upon prior offenses, § 17.545 promoted and furthered retribution against sex offenders for their past crimes. A registrant could not establish a permanent home. The statute was connected to public safety. However, its inherent flaws prevented that connection from being "rational." The magnitude of restraint involved in residency restrictions was sufficient for a lack of individual assessment to render the statute punitive. Given the drastic consequences of Kentucky's residency restrictions, and the fact that there was no individual determination of the threat a particular registrant posed to public safety, § 17.545 was excessive with respect to the nonpunitive purpose of public safety. Section 17.545 was excessive with respect to the purpose of public safety.


OUTCOME: The law was certified.

 

134 of 1068 DOCUMENTS


Helton v. Commonwealth


2008-SC-000141-MR


SUPREME COURT OF KENTUCKY


299 S.W.3d 555; 2009 Ky. LEXIS 186



August 27, 2009, Rendered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication January 21, 2010.
Rehearing denied by Helton v. Commonwealth, 2010 Ky. LEXIS 549 (Ky., Jan. 21, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
ON APPEAL FROM JESSAMINE CIRCUIT COURT. HONORABLE C. HUNTER DAUGHERTY, JUDGE. NO. 06-CR-00275.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the Jessamine Circuit Court (Kentucky) that convicted her of multiple counts of wanton murder and several other charges stemming from a car accident in which she had been driving while under the influence of alcohol, arguing that the trial court erred in denying her motion to suppress blood-alcohol evidence where the blood sample was taken at the hospital by the police while she was unconscious.


OVERVIEW: After drinking with a friend, defendant was driving home when she was involved in an accident that led to the death of four of her passengers. Defendant was transported to a hospital where deputies took a blood sample without a warrant and without her consent. After her suppression motion was denied, defendant pled guilty to the charges against her but reserved her right to contest the trial court's ruling on her motion. On review, the court held that, under the implied consent law, Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 189A.103, drivers in Kentucky consented to alcohol concentration testing, and the fact that defendant was unconscious did not constitute a withdrawal of her consent. However, before a blood alcohol test could be performed, the deputies were required to have probable cause to believe that alcohol was involved in the wreck. If the blood test was done merely because there were fatalities, the lack of probable cause rendered the testing unlawful under the Fourth Amendment, U.S. Const. amend. IV. Because the trial court did not engage in a sufficient analysis to determine whether probable cause existed, its denial of defendant's suppression motion could not stand.


OUTCOME: The judgment of conviction was vacated, and the case was remanded for a new suppression hearing to determine whether the evidence established that the police had probable cause to believe that alcohol played a role in the accident.

Crossland v. Commonwealth


2007-SC-000689-MR


SUPREME COURT OF KENTUCKY


291 S.W.3d 223; 2009 Ky. LEXIS 190



August 27, 2009, Rendered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication September 17, 2009.
Related proceeding at Crossland v. Commonwealth, 2010 Ky. App. Unpub. LEXIS 917 (Ky. Ct. App., Dec. 3, 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
ON APPEAL FROM McCRACKEN CIRCUIT COURT. HONORABLE R. JEFFREY HINES, JUDGE. NO. 06-CR-00029.
Crossland v. Commonwealth, 2008 Ky. App. Unpub. LEXIS 729 (Ky. Ct. App., Oct. 17, 2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a judgment of the McCracken Circuit Court (Kentucky) that convicted him of numerous criminal offenses, arguing that the trial court erred by substituting an alternate juror for a juror who was excused after the case had been submitted to the jury for deliberation.


OVERVIEW: Shortly after the case was submitted to the jury, the trial judge announced that one of the jurors had previously been excused by the chief judge. Although the alternate juror had been excused, the trial court ordered that he be located and return to join the already-deliberating jury. On review, the court held that reinstating a discharged alternate juror during deliberations, absent defendant's consent, required reversal of a conviction only when defendant was prejudiced by the procedure. However, assurance that the alternate juror remained qualified to serve was a prerequisite to substitution and, unless waived by defendant, had to be established on the record. Because the trial court failed to do so, the court was left to speculate about whether the recalled juror was free of outside taint. Further, the trial court erred in failing to instruct the newly reconstituted jury to begin its deliberations anew once the alternate juror was added to the panel. Absent such an instruction, the court could not determine whether the jury began its deliberations anew or whether the new juror simply acceded to a decision already made by the original jurors during prior deliberations.


OUTCOME: The judgment of conviction was reversed, and the case was remanded for a new trial.

 

136 of 1068 DOCUMENTS


Owens v. Commonwealth


2006-SC-000037-MR


SUPREME COURT OF KENTUCKY


291 S.W.3d 704; 2009 Ky. LEXIS 258



August 27, 2009, Rendered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication September 17, 2009.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
ON APPEAL FROM TAYLOR CIRCUIT COURT. HONORABLE DOUGHLAS M. GEORGE, JUDGE. NO. 04-CR-00225.
Owens v. Kentucky, 129 S. Ct. 2155, 173 L. Ed. 2d 1152, 2009 U.S. LEXIS 3314 (U.S., 2009)



DISPOSITION: AFFIRMING.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Taylor Circuit Court, Kentucky, denied defendant's motion to suppress and found defendant guilty of all charges, recommending a sentence of twelve months with a $500 fine for the possession of marijuana conviction, and a twenty-year sentence for the first-degree persistent felony offender (PFO) conviction. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant argued that the officers overstepped constitutional bounds when they frisked him for weapons. The supreme court noted that once the driver of the vehicle was lawfully arrested, the officer had the authority to search the passenger compartment of the vehicle. An officer could order a passenger to exit the vehicle pending completion of a minor traffic stop. An officer could order a passenger to exit a vehicle while that vehicle was searched incident to the lawful arrest of the driver. Under the automatic companion rule, all companions of the arrestee within the immediate vicinity, capable of accomplishing a harmful assault on the officer, were constitutionally subjected to the cursory pat-down reasonably necessary to give assurance that they were unarmed. The supreme court found that adoption of the automatic companion rule protected the officer, and was designed to apply only in situations in which the driver of a vehicle has been lawfully arrested and the passengers had been lawfully expelled in preparation for a lawful search of the vehicle. Only in those limited circumstances could an officer conduct a brief pat-down for weapons of the vehicle's passengers.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

 

137 of 1068 DOCUMENTS


Miller v. Johnson Controls, Inc.


2006-SC-000416-DG, 2007-SC-000819-DG


SUPREME COURT OF KENTUCKY


296 S.W.3d 392; 2009 Ky. LEXIS 196



August 27, 2009, Rendered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication November 25, 2009
Rehearing denied by Miller v. Johnson Controls, Inc., 2009 Ky. LEXIS 537 (Ky., Nov. 25, 2009)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by Johnson Controls, Inc. v. Miller, 130 S. Ct. 3324, 176 L. Ed. 2d 1240, 2010 U.S. LEXIS 4206 (U.S., 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
ON REVIEW FROM COURT OF APPEALS CASE NO. 2004-CA-001566-MR. FRANKLIN CIRCUIT COURT NOS. 00-CI-00523 AND 00-CI-00661.
Johnson Controls, Inc. v. Rudolph, 2006 Ky. App. LEXIS 132 (Ky. Ct. App., May 5, 2006)



DISPOSITION:  The decision of the court of appeals was reversed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, the Kentucky Department of Revenue, challenged a decision of the Court of Appeals of Kentucky that held that amendments to Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. §§ 141.120 and 141.200 were unenforceable in that they violated the due process rights of appellee corporations.


OVERVIEW: The corporations originally filed separate state tax returns. After a ruling that related corporations could file a combined tax return under the unitary business concept, the corporations substituted combined returns. Because they owed less tax under this approach and would be entitled to tax refunds and because the adoption of this practice by a multitude of related corporations would result in significant revenue loss to the state, the legislature amended Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. §§ 141.120 and 141.200 to bar combined returns and to deny refunds for amended combined returns. The corporations challenged the amendments, arguing that they were deprived of a vested property interest in the tax refunds. On review, the court held that, because the amendments made to the Kentucky tax code were made to clarify the law regarding combined returns under the unitary business plan and to exercise the legislature's revenue raising power, the amendments furthered a legitimate government interests and were rationally related to that purpose. As such, the corporations' due process rights were not affected by the amendments' retroactive application, and they were not entitled to refunds.


OUTCOME: The decision of the court of appeals was reversed.

Gibson v. Commonwealth


2007-SC-000481-DG


SUPREME COURT OF KENTUCKY


291 S.W.3d 686; 2009 Ky. LEXIS 155



June 25, 2009, Rendered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication October 1, 2009
Rehearing denied by Gibson v. Commonwealth, 2009 Ky. LEXIS 389 (Ky., Oct. 1, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
ON REVIEW FROM COURT OF APPEALS. CASE NO. 2006-CA-001147. GRAYSON CIRCUIT COURT NO. 03-CR-00119.
Gibson v. Commonwealth, 2007 Ky. App. LEXIS 178 (Ky. Ct. App., June 8, 2007)



DISPOSITION: Accordingly, the decision of the Court of Appeals is affirmed.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was charged with theft of services over $ 300.00, in violation of Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 514.050. The circuit court granted the motion of the Commonwealth of Kentucky to dismiss the charge without prejudice. Defendant filed a motion to amend the order of dismissal, asking that the dismissal be deemed to be with prejudice. The court denied the motion. The Court of Appeals of Kentucky affirmed the decision. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: The essence of the offense was that defendant unlawfully received electricity for her business by diverting it through an illegally placed, hidden wire from a neighboring electric meter. She argued that the trial judge abused his discretion by refusing to convert the dismissal from without prejudice to with prejudice. She claimed that she was prejudiced by the dismissal because she was then unable to demand a trial that would clear her name, and she was unable to have the arrest expunged from her record. The state supreme court found that Ky. R. Civ. P. 41.01 could not be extended to criminal cases as Ky. R. Crim. P. 9.64 covered the matter of voluntary dismissals of criminal cases. Further, the requested dismissal with prejudice would have violated the separation of powers doctrine embodied in Ky. Const. §§ 27 and 28. Finally, it was not within the province of the circuit court, under Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 505.030, to grant defendant's request to designate the dismissal with prejudice because she made no claim of the deprivation of a statutory right or a principle of Constitutional law which foreclosed a future attempt to prosecute her.


OUTCOME: The decision of the intermediate appellate court was affirmed.

Rodgers v. Commonwealth


2007-SC-000040-MR


SUPREME COURT OF KENTUCKY


285 S.W.3d 740; 2009 Ky. LEXIS 163



June 25, 2009, Rendered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication July 16, 2009.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
ON APPEAL FROM JEFFERSON CIRCUIT COURT. HONORABLE W. DOUGLAS KEMPER, JUDGE. CASE NOS. 00-CR-002529 AND 00-CR-002671.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Following a jury trial, defendant was convicted of first-degree manslaughter. The Jefferson Circuit Court (Kentucky) sentenced defendant as a second-degree persistent felon to twenty years in prison. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: The supreme court first concluded that the Commonwealth's use of the post-arrest statements of defendant and his codefendant did not entitle defendant to a separate trial and did not infringe upon his right to present a defense. Codefendant's statement was redacted to remove any reference to defendant. The limited introduction of defendant's own statement did not infringe on defendant's right to present a defense, as the portion not admitted was not critical to his defense. The supreme court further concluded that defendant's self-defense claim was correctly presented to the jury according to the law as it existed at the time of defendant's offense, and his claim of immunity under newly enacted Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 503.085, which applied only prospectively, was properly denied. Finally, the supreme court concluded that the jury was fairly selected and was correctly instructed with respect to defendant's right not to testify, his presumed innocence, and the Commonwealth's burden of proof. Among other things, the trial court properly rejected defendant's fair cross section challenge, as he made no attempt to show that African-Americans were regularly underrepresented.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

Caneyville Volunteer Fire Dep't v. Green's Motorcycle Salvage, Inc.


2007-SC-000517-DG


SUPREME COURT OF KENTUCKY


286 S.W.3d 790; 2009 Ky. LEXIS 158



June 25, 2009, Rendered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication July 16, 2009.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
ON REVIEW FROM COURT OF APPEALS. NO. 2006-CA-001142-MR. GRAYSON CIRCUIT COURT NO. 05-CI-00480.
Green's Motorcycle Salvage, Inc. v. Caneyville Volunteer Fire Dep't, 2007 Ky. App. LEXIS 197 (Ky. Ct. App., June 29, 2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellee property owners sued appellants, the fire department, the fire chief, and the city, for negligence regarding a fire on the owners' property. The trial court dismissed the case on grounds of immunity. The Court of Appeals of Kentucky reversed the trial court as to the fire department and the city. The intermediate appellate court also remanded the matter of the fire chief's qualified official immunity. Discretionary review was granted.


OVERVIEW: The owners alleged that the fire department, its fire chief, and the city were negligent in failing to timely extinguish a fire and that the owners suffered more severe property damage than they otherwise would have if additional measures had been taken to extinguish the fire. The state supreme court found that Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. §§ 75.070 and 95.830(2) were constitutional under Ky. Const. §§ 230 and 231 and that they did not violate the jural rights doctrine under Ky. Const. §§ 14, 54, and 241. Further, the city was entitled to dismissal because the fire department was an agent of the Commonwealth of Kentucky, having been recognized as such by Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 75.070 and declared immune from suit in tort. Because fire departments were thus immune from suit in tort, and were agents of the Commonwealth, albeit operating on a local basis, there was no attendant municipality liability for the fire department's firefighting actions. This was also consistent with Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 95.830(2). Additionally, the fire chief was immune in his official capacity. However, in his individual capacity, he was entitled to qualified official immunity for his discretionary acts.


OUTCOME: The decision of the intermediate appellate court was reversed, and the decision of the trial court, albeit, in some instances, for different reasons was affirmed.

Sanderson v. Commonwealth


2007-SC-000537-MR


SUPREME COURT OF KENTUCKY


291 S.W.3d 610; 2009 Ky. LEXIS 96



May 21, 2009, Rendered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication October 1, 2009
Rehearing denied by Sanderson v. Commonwealth, 2009 Ky. LEXIS 388 (Ky., Oct. 1, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
ON APPEAL FROM GRAVES CIRCUIT COURT. HONORABLE TIMOTHY C. STARK, JUDGE. NO. 06-CR-00189.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the judgment of the Graves Circuit Court (Kentucky), which convicted him of two counts of second-degree sodomy and three counts of first-degree sexual abuse. Defendant raised various issues on appeal, including his contention that the trial court improperly admitted testimony about Child Sexual Abuse Accommodation Syndrome (CSAAS) from various witnesses.


OVERVIEW: The child victim alleged that defendant, her step-father, sexually abused her on a weekly basis for a period of six years. The court held that the trial court erred when it allowed a clinical psychologist to testify that sexually abused children, like the victim, commonly added details over time through counseling and erred in allowing the psychologist to identify generic characteristics of child sex abuse victims by describing them as outwardly appearing happy as this testimony did not answer the question of whether other children who had not been similarly abused might also develop the same symptoms or traits. This testimony was not relevant under Ky. R. Evid. 401; thus, the admission of CSAAS testimony was improper and required the reversal of defendant's conviction. The trial court also erred in sentencing defendant to 35 years in prison rather than the maximum twenty-year sentence under Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. §§ 532.110(1)(c) and 532.080(6)(b) as the 35-year-sentence exceeded in length the longest extended term authorized by Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 532.080 for the Class C felony conviction of second-degree sodomy, the highest class of crimes of which defendant was convicted.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the trial court and remanded the cause for a new trial.

 

142 of 1068 DOCUMENTS


Tobar v. Commonwealth


2007-SC-000842-DG


SUPREME COURT OF KENTUCKY


284 S.W.3d 133; 2009 Ky. LEXIS 81



May 21, 2009, Rendered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication June 11, 2009.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
ON REVIEW FROM COURT OF APPEALS. CASE NO. 2006-CA-001314-MR. FAYETTE CIRCUIT COURT NO. 05-CR-01640.
Tobar v. Commonwealth, 2007 Ky. App. LEXIS 396 (Ky. Ct. App., Oct. 12, 2007)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After the trial court denied defendant's motion to dismiss, defendant entered a conditional guilty plea to the offense of failure to comply with sex offender registration, Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 17.510(10)(a), and was sentenced to a term of one year imprisonment, probated for five years. The Court of Appeals of Kentucky affirmed the conviction. Defendant appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was expelled from a center where he had been living, and became homeless. Defendant made no attempt to report to the proper authorities when he moved away from the center. Defendant argued that Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 17.510(10)(a) was unconstitutionally vague as applied to him because he was homeless when charged and thus unable to register a change in address. The appellate court found that the fact that defendant became homeless was irrelevant to the statute's application. The focus of § 17.510(10)(a) was that any change in a sex offender's address be reported to the proper authorities. Nowhere in the plain language of the statute did it require that the registrant must have had an actual place he was moving to. Thus § 17.510(10)(a) was not void for vagueness as applied to defendant. There was no reason why defendant could not have reported to the proper authorities that he no longer lived at the center. Further, § 17.510(10)(a) as written did not cause arbitrary or discriminatory enforcement and did not criminalize being homeless. All sex offenders, regardless of socioeconomic status, had to register with the proper authorities and report any change in address.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Commonwealth v. McBride


2005-SC-000539-DG, 2005-SC-000930-DG


SUPREME COURT OF KENTUCKY


281 S.W.3d 799; 2009 Ky. LEXIS 52



April 23, 2009, Rendered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Corrected by Commonwealth v. McBride, 2009 Ky. LEXIS 102 (Ky., Apr. 27, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY: Released for Publication May 14, 2009. [**1] 
ON REVIEW FROM COURT OF APPEALS. CASE NO. 2003-CA-001843-MR. MONTGOMERY CIRCUIT COURT NO. 01-CR-00069.
McBride v. Commonwealth, 2005 Ky. App. Unpub. LEXIS 776 (Ky. Ct. App., June 10, 2005)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant was convicted of failure to register as a sex offender in Kentucky, under former Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 17.510(7), based on his conviction of a sex crime in Tennessee and subsequent failure to register in Kentucky, upon moving to Kentucky. The Court of Appeals (Kentucky) reversed the conviction, and the Commonwealth appealed.


OVERVIEW: Defendant's conviction was reversed because he was not notified to register. The supreme court held he was properly convicted because he had to register in Tennessee and, thus, was subject to former Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 17.510(7). A failure to notify him, under former Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 17.510(6), was irrelevant because Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 17.510(7) imposed an absolute duty to register, once he moved to Kentucky, and did not require the Commonwealth to notify him of this duty. The statute was not unconstitutionally vague because it sufficiently notified him that, if he did not register, he would be guilty of a crime. If evidence that he was a registered sex offender in Tennessee was hearsay, admitting it was harmless because other admissible evidence proved this. The erroneous admission of testimony that he worked in Kentucky was harmless because other witnesses confirmed it. The fact that he was convicted of a sex crime before the effective date of Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 17.510(11), as amended in 2000, did not cause the trial court to lack jurisdiction to convict him of a felony because he did not have to register until he moved to Kentucky, after the statute was amended.


OUTCOME: The decision of the court of appeals was reversed, and defendant's conviction was reinstated.

Cain v. Lodestar Energy, Inc.


2008-SC-000178-WC


SUPREME COURT OF KENTUCKY


302 S.W.3d 39; 2009 Ky. LEXIS 71



March 19, 2009, Rendered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication August 27, 2009.
Rehearing denied by Cain v. Lodestar Energy, Inc., 2009 Ky. LEXIS 332 (Ky., Aug. 27, 2009)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
ON APPEAL FROM COURT OF APPEALS. CASE NO. 2007-CA-000010-WC. WORKERS' COMPENSATION BOARD NO. 02-01726.
Cain v. Lodestar Energy, Inc., 2008 Ky. App. Unpub. LEXIS 1186 (Ky. Ct. App., Feb. 22, 2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant, a workers' compensation benefits claimant, challenged an order of the Court of Appeals (Kentucky), which affirmed the decision of appellees, an administrative law judge (ALJ) and the Workers' Compensation Board, to dismiss his retraining incentive benefit (RIB) claim because he failed to rebut the consensus of a panel of three "B" readers that the best quality x-ray in evidence showed only category 0/0 disease.


OVERVIEW: The claimant filed an application for benefits, alleging that he suffered from coal workers' pneumoconiosis, but he did not allege a respiratory impairment. The claimant and his employer filed a chest x-ray and a "B" reader's interpretation of the x-ray. The Office of Workers' Claims determined that the parties' reports were not in consensus and pursuant to Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 342.316(3)(b)(4)(e), hired three "B" readers to choose and interpret the best quality x-ray. The panel reached a consensus of category 0. In rebuttal, the claimant filed another interpretation of the x-ray that he submitted initially. The ALJ dismissed the claim. The supreme court held that § 342.316(3)(b)(4)(e) denied the claimant equal protection because it discriminated between him and a similarly-situated worker whose employer also submitted evidence of category 1 disease but whose claim was not subject to the second phase of the consensus process. Section 342.316(3)(b)(4)(e) created two classes of workers based solely on the amount of discrepancy between the worker's and employer's evidence. Therefore, it denied equal protection under both the 14th Amendment and Ky. Const. §§ 1, 2, and 3.


OUTCOME: The supreme court affirmed the decision of the court of appeals in part, reversed the decision in part, and remanded the claim to the ALJ for further consideration based on the evidence that the parties submitted in the initial phase of the consensus process.

Lexington-Fayette Urban County Gov't v. Johnson


2007-SC-000294-DG


SUPREME COURT OF KENTUCKY


280 S.W.3d 31; 2009 Ky. LEXIS 76

NOTICE: TO BE PUBLISHED

PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
ON REVIEW FROM COURT OF APPEALS. CASE NO. 2006-CA-000124-MR AND 2006-CA-000191-MR. FAYETTE CIRCUIT COURT NO. 00-CI-03636.
Lexington-Fayette Urban County Gov't v. Johnson, 2008 Ky. LEXIS 46 (Ky., Mar. 12, 2008)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellee retirees sued appellant county, seeking health insurance coverage under former Lexington-Fayette Urban County Government, Ky., Ordinance No. 217-99 (1999) and challenging the constitutionality of an amendment to that ordinance. The Court of Appeals of Kentucky affirmed the trial court's ruling that the retirees were covered under the prior ordinance, and held that the amended version was unconstitutional. The county appealed.


OVERVIEW: The retirees opted out of health coverage when they retired. The issues were whether former Lexington-Fayette Urban County Government, Ky., Ordinance No. 217-99 (1999) provided an opportunity for them to opt back into coverage, and whether the amended version of that ordinance, Lexington-Fayette Urban County Government, Ky., Ordinance No. 217-99 (2000), which excluded the retirees from coverage, was valid. The appellate court found, contrary to the retirees' claim, that former Lexington-Fayette Urban County Government, Ky., Ordinance No. 217-99(c) (1999) was meant to exclude someone, otherwise it would have been meaningless. The amendments showed clearly that only those participants who had not opted out of the plan before the time the 1999 ordinance became effective, July 1, 1999, were eligible. The protective cross-appeal in the intermediate appellate court concerned the constitutionality of the 2000 ordinance, which became moot with this interpretation of the 1999 ordinance.


OUTCOME: The decision of the intermediate appellate court on the cross-appeal was vacated, and the cross-appeal was dismissed.

Louisville/Jefferson County Metro Gov't v. City of Prospect


2006-SC-000903-DG


SUPREME COURT OF KENTUCKY


277 S.W.3d 227; 2009 Ky. LEXIS 6



January 22, 2009, Rendered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication February 12, 2009.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
ON REVIEW FROM COURT OF APPEALS. CASE NOS. 2005-CA-001235-MR AND 2005-CA-001305-MR. JEFFERSON CIRCUIT COURT NO. 02-CI-006764.
Louisville/Jefferson County Metro Gov't v. City of Prospect, 2006 Ky. App. LEXIS 326 (Ky. Ct. App., Nov. 3, 2006)


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant metro government as successor to a first-class city appealed a judgment by the Court of Appeals (Kentucky) that gave annexation priority to a fourth-class city.


OVERVIEW: Both the first-class city and the fourth-class city sought to annex the property in question. Although the first-class city's annexation ordinance (first reading) was first in time, the trial court ruled the delay in completing the annexation was unreasonable, and that reintroduction of the first reading every six months did not relate back to the first reading. The Court of Appeals agreed that the true first reading was after the reintroduction requirement for annexation ordinances was removed. Therefore, the first-class city's proposed annexation ordinance lost priority over the fourth-class city's ordinance. The state supreme court found, inter alia, that because the first-class city had a cooperative agreement in effect, Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 81A.005 gave it priority to annex the property in question. In addition, Ky. Rev. Stat. Ann. § 79.310, as amended, allowed the first-class city 22 years to complete the annexation process. Because that statutory period had not expired before the fourth-class city's ordinance was enacted, its annexation ordinance was void.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed, and the fourth-class city's annexation ordinance was declared void.

Cardine v. Commonwealth


2006-SC-000677-MR, 2006-SC-000680-MR


SUPREME COURT OF KENTUCKY


283 S.W.3d 641; 2009 Ky. LEXIS 7



January 22, 2009, Rendered


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: Released for Publication June 25, 2009.
Rehearing denied by Cardine v. Commonwealth, 2009 Ky. LEXIS 398 (Ky., June 25, 2009)
Rehearing denied by Curry v. Commonwealth, 2009 Ky. LEXIS 401 (Ky., June 25, 2009)
US Supreme Court certiorari denied by, Motion granted by Kentucky v. Cardine, 130 S. Ct. 1879, 176 L. Ed. 2d 399, 2010 U.S. LEXIS 2451 (U.S., 2010)


PRIOR HISTORY:  [**1] 
ON APPEAL FROM JEFFERSON CIRCUIT COURT. HONORABLE DENISE CLAYTON, JUDGE. NOS. 04-CR-001803-001 AND 04-CR-001803-002.



DISPOSITION: REVERSING


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: When defendants first went to trial, the trial court declared a mistrial soon after the jury was sworn. After a second joint jury trial, defendants were found guilty of murder, attempted murder, and assault in the second degree. The Jefferson Circuit Court (Kentucky) sentenced defendants to 30 years' imprisonment. Defendants appealed.


OVERVIEW: First defendant argued that the second trial violated his double jeopardy rights under Ky. Const. § 13, and U.S. Const. Amends. V and XIV. The supreme court agreed, holding that jeopardy attached when the jury was sworn for the first trial. The supreme court also found no showing of manifest necessity supporting the grant of a mistrial. The supreme court found that the proffered newly discovered evidence, a witness who would testify that second defendant tried to sell him a gun on the day of the shooting, was cumulative. The Commonwealth already had two witnesses to testify that they had made earlier statements to police that second defendant shot at them. The supreme court further concluded that the relative weight of the Commonwealth's late-discovered witness merited only exclusion or a continuance, not a mistrial. While second defendant did not raise a double jeopardy objection in his brief, the supreme court addressed the issue under the palpable error rule, and found a such error, making his conviction a violation of double jeopardy as well. Contrary to the Commonwealth's claim, defendants were not required to object to the mistrial to preserve the issue for review.


OUTCOME: Defendant's convictions were reversed.
